class="mw-indicator" id="mw-indicator-pp-default"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Protection_policy#semi" title="This article is semi-protected."><img alt="Page semi-protected" data-file-height="512" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Semi-protection-shackle.svg/40px-Semi-protection-shackle.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Nuclear power</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Atomic power" redirects here. For the film, see <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Power_(film)" title="Atomic Power (film)">Atomic Power (film)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2377" data-file-width="3328" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg/220px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg/330px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg/440px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1200 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MWe" title="MWe">MWe</a> <a href="/wiki/Leibstadt_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Leibstadt Nuclear Power Plant">Leibstadt Nuclear Power Plant</a> in Switzerland. The <a href="/wiki/Boiling_water_reactor" title="Boiling water reactor">boiling water reactor</a> (BWR), located inside the dome capped cylindrical structure, is dwarfed in size by its <a href="/wiki/Cooling_tower" title="Cooling tower">cooling tower</a>. The station produces a yearly average of 25 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kilowatt-hours</a> per day, sufficient to power a city the size of <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg/220px-PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg/330px-PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg/440px-PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Palo_Verde_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Palo Verde Nuclear Generating Station">Palo Verde Nuclear Generating Station</a>, the largest in the United States with 3 <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">pressurized water reactors</a> (PWRs), is situated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arizona_desert" title="Arizona desert">Arizona desert</a>. It uses <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a> from cities as its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooling_water" title="Cooling water">cooling water</a> in 9 squat mechanical draft cooling towers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Its total <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spent_fuel" title="Spent fuel">spent fuel</a> inventory, produced since 1986, is contained in <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry cask storage</a> cylinders located between the artificial body of water and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_switchyard" title="Electrical switchyard">electrical switchyard</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USS_Enterprise_(CVAN-65),_USS_Long_Beach_(CGN-9)_and_USS_Bainbridge_(DLGN-25)_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit,_in_1964.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="786" data-file-width="1062" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg/220px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg/330px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg/440px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USS_Enterprise_(CVAN-65),_USS_Long_Beach_(CGN-9)_and_USS_Bainbridge_(DLGN-25)_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit,_in_1964.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. nuclear powered ships: (top to bottom) cruisers <a href="/wiki/USS_Bainbridge_(CGN-25)" title="USS Bainbridge (CGN-25)">USS&#160;<i>Bainbridge</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Long_Beach_(CGN-9)" title="USS Long Beach (CGN-9)">USS&#160;<i>Long Beach</i></a>, and <a href="/wiki/USS_Enterprise_(CVN-65)" title="USS Enterprise (CVN-65)">USS&#160;<i>Enterprise</i></a>, the first nuclear-powered <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a>. Picture taken in 1964 during a record setting voyage of 26,540&#160;nmi (49,152&#160;km) around the world in 65 days without refueling. Crew members are spelling out <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass-energy_equivalence" title="Mass-energy equivalence">mass-energy equivalence</a> formula <i>E&#160;=&#160;mc<sup>2</sup></i> on the <a href="/wiki/Flight_deck" title="Flight deck">flight deck</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_electricity_generation_by_source_pie_chart.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/World_electricity_generation_by_source_pie_chart.svg/220px-World_electricity_generation_by_source_pie_chart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/World_electricity_generation_by_source_pie_chart.svg/330px-World_electricity_generation_by_source_pie_chart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/World_electricity_generation_by_source_pie_chart.svg/440px-World_electricity_generation_by_source_pie_chart.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_electricity_generation_by_source_pie_chart.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption#Electricity_generation" title="World energy consumption">Global civilian electricity generation</a> by source. Some 23,816 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TWh" title="TWh">TWh</a> total.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA-Report-keyworld-2015_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-Report-keyworld-2015-4">[4]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p><b>Nuclear power</b> is the use of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a> that release <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">nuclear energy</a> to generate heat, which most frequently is then used in <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbines</a> to produce electricity in a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plant</a>. As a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_technology" title="Nuclear technology">nuclear technology</a>, nuclear power can be obtained from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_decay" title="Nuclear decay">nuclear decay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> reactions.\nPresently, the vast majority of electricity from nuclear power is produced by nuclear fission of <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>.\nNuclear decay processes are used in niche applications such as <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a>.\nGenerating electricity from <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">fusion power</a> remains at the focus of international research.\nThis article mostly deals with nuclear fission power for electricity generation.\n</p><p>Civilian nuclear power supplied 2,488 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatt_hour" title="Terawatt hour">terawatt hours</a> (TWh) of electricity in 2017, equivalent to about 10% of <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption#Electricity_generation" title="World energy consumption">global electricity generation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaea_pris_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-iaea_pris-5">[5]</a></sup>\nAs of April 2018, there are <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">449 civilian fission reactors in the world</a>, with a combined electrical capacity of 394 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">gigawatt</a> (GW).\nAs of 2018, there are 58 <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor#Classification_by_use" title="Nuclear reactor">power reactors</a> under construction and 154 reactors planned, with a combined capacity of 63 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> and 157 GW, respectively. As of January 2019, 337 more reactors were proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNA-6">[6]</a></sup>\nMost reactors under construction are <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">generation III reactors</a> in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaea_pris_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-iaea_pris-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nuclear power is classified as a low <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> energy supply technology, along with <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>, by the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Since its commercialization in the 1970s, nuclear power has prevented about 1.84 million air pollution-related deaths and the emission of about 64 billion tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">carbon dioxide equivalent</a> that would have otherwise resulted from the burning of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889&#8211;4895_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889&#8211;4895-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">debate about nuclear power</a>.\nProponents, such as the <a href="/wiki/World_Nuclear_Association" title="World Nuclear Association">World Nuclear Association</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmentalists_for_Nuclear_Energy" title="Environmentalists for Nuclear Energy">Environmentalists for Nuclear Energy</a>, contend that nuclear power is a safe, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">sustainable energy</a> source that reduces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a>.\n<a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">Opponents</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Information_and_Resource_Service" title="Nuclear Information and Resource Service">NIRS</a>, contend that nuclear power poses many threats to <a href="/wiki/Environmental_radioactivity" title="Environmental radioactivity">people and the environment</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents and incidents">Accidents in nuclear power plants</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a> in the Soviet Union in 1986, the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a> in Japan in 2011, and the more contained <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> in the United States in 1979.\nThere have also been some nuclear submarine accidents.\nNuclear reactors have caused the lowest number of fatalities per unit of energy generated when compared to fossil fuels and hydropower.\nCoal, petroleum, natural gas and hydroelectricity each have caused a greater number of fatalities per unit of energy, due to air pollution and <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">accidents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarkandyaWilkinson2007_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarkandyaWilkinson2007-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Collaboration on research and development towards greater efficiency, safety and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">recycling of spent fuel</a> in future <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">generation IV reactors</a> presently includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euratom" title="Euratom">Euratom</a> and the co-operation of more than 10 permanent member countries globally.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" proliferation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Environmental_impact"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Environmental impact</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Carbon_emissions"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Carbon emissions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#Radiation"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Radiation</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Renewable_energy_and_nuclear_power"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Renewable energy and nuclear power</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Debate_on_nuclear_power"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Debate on nuclear power</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Research"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Research</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Advanced_fission_reactor_designs"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Advanced fission reactor designs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Hybrid_nuclear_fusion-fission"><span class="tocnumber">12.2</span> <span class="toctext">Hybrid nuclear fusion-fission</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Nuclear_fusion"><span class="tocnumber">12.3</span> <span class="toctext">Nuclear fusion</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="441" data-file-width="671" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/220px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/330px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/440px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">Nuclear binding energy</a> of all natural elements in the periodic table. Higher values translate into more tightly bound nuclei and greater nuclear stability. <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Iron</a> (Fe) is the end product of <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> within the core of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> fusing stars. The elements surrounding iron are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_products" title="Fission products">fission products</a> of the fissionable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actinides" title="Actinides">actinides</a> (e.g. uranium). Except for iron, all other elemental nuclei have in theory the potential to be nuclear fuel, and the greater distance from iron the greater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_potential_energy" title="Nuclear potential energy">nuclear potential energy</a> that could be released.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission#History" title="Nuclear fission">Nuclear fission &#167;&#160;History</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Age" title="Atomic Age">Atomic Age</a></div>\n<p>In 1932 physicist <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> discovered that when lithium atoms were "split" by protons from a proton accelerator, immense amounts of energy were released in accordance with the principle of <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass&#8211;energy equivalence">mass&#8211;energy equivalence</a>. However, he and other nuclear physics pioneers <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> believed harnessing the power of the atom for practical purposes anytime in the near future was unlikely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The same year, his doctoral student <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a> discovered the <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> which was immediately recognized as a potential tool for nuclear experimentation because of its lack of an electric charge. Experiments bombarding materials with neutrons led <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Joliot-Curie" title="Fr&#233;d&#233;ric Joliot-Curie">Fr&#233;d&#233;ric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Ir&#232;ne Joliot-Curie">Ir&#232;ne Joliot-Curie</a> to discover <a href="/wiki/Induced_radioactivity" title="Induced radioactivity">induced radioactivity</a> in 1934, which allowed the creation of radium-like elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Further work by <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Enrico Fermi</a> in the 1930s focused on using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slow_neutron" title="Slow neutron">slow neutrons</a> to increase the effectiveness of induced radioactivity. Experiments bombarding uranium with neutrons led Fermi to believe he had created a new, transuranic element, which was dubbed <a href="/wiki/Hesperium" title="Hesperium">hesperium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Np_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Np-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1938, German chemists <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Strassmann" title="Fritz Strassmann">Fritz Strassmann</a>, along with Austrian physicist <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and Meitner\'s nephew, <a href="/wiki/Otto_Robert_Frisch" title="Otto Robert Frisch">Otto Robert Frisch</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> conducted experiments with the products of neutron-bombarded uranium, as a means of further investigating Fermi\'s claims.\nThey determined that the relatively tiny neutron split the nucleus of the massive uranium atoms into two roughly equal pieces, contradicting Fermi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Np_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Np-13">[13]</a></sup>\nThis was an extremely surprising result: all other forms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_decay" title="Nuclear decay">nuclear decay</a> involved only small changes to the mass of the nucleus, whereas this process&#8212;dubbed "fission" as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_fission" title="Binary fission">reference to biology</a>&#8212;involved a complete rupture of the nucleus.\nNumerous scientists, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3_Szil%C3%A1rd" title="Le&#243; Szil&#225;rd">Le&#243; Szil&#225;rd</a>, who was one of the first, recognized that if fission reactions released additional neutrons, a self-sustaining <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a> could result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inside_the_Atomic_Patent_Office_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inside_the_Atomic_Patent_Office-18">[18]</a></sup> Once this was experimentally confirmed and announced by Fr&#233;d&#233;ric Joliot-Curie in 1939, scientists in many countries (including the United States, the United Kingdom, France, Germany, and the Soviet Union) petitioned their governments for support of nuclear fission research, just on the cusp of World War II, for the development of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_nuclear_reactor">First nuclear reactor</span></h3>\n<p>In the United States, where Fermi and Szil&#225;rd had both emigrated, the discovery of the nuclear chain reaction led to the creation of the first man-made reactor, the  <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor#Classification_by_use" title="Nuclear reactor">research reactor</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Pile-1" title="Chicago Pile-1">Chicago Pile-1</a>, which achieved self-sustaining power/<a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">criticality</a> on December 2, 1942. The reactor\'s development was part of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> effort to create <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bombs</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. It led to the building of larger single-purpose <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor#Classification_by_use" title="Nuclear reactor">production reactors</a>, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-10_Pile" title="X-10 Pile">X-10 Pile</a>, for the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weapons-grade_plutonium" title="Weapons-grade plutonium">weapons-grade plutonium</a> for use in the first nuclear weapons. The United States tested the first nuclear weapon in July 1945, the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)" title="Trinity (nuclear test)">Trinity test</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a> taking place one month later.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/220px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/330px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/440px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first light bulbs ever lit by electricity generated by nuclear power at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EBR-1" title="EBR-1">EBR-1</a> at <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory</a>-West, December 20, 1951.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>  As the first <a href="/wiki/Liquid_metal_cooled_reactor" title="Liquid metal cooled reactor">liquid metal cooled fast reactor</a>, it demonstrated Fermi\'s Experimental <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">fuel Breeding</a> Reactor principle, to maximize the usable energy obtainable from the, initially considered, scarce <a href="/wiki/Natural_uranium" title="Natural uranium">natural uranium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In August 1945, the first widely distributed account of nuclear energy, in the form of the pocketbook <i>The Atomic Age</i>, discussed the peaceful future uses of nuclear energy and depicted a future where fossil fuels would go unused.\nNobel laureate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glenn_Seaborg" title="Glenn Seaborg">Glenn Seaborg</a>, who later chaired the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">Atomic Energy Commission</a>, is quoted as saying "there will be <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_thermal_rocket" title="Nuclear thermal rocket">nuclear powered earth-to-moon shuttles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238#Nuclear_powered_pacemakers" title="Plutonium-238">nuclear powered artificial hearts</a>, plutonium heated swimming pools for SCUBA divers, and much more".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the same month, with the end of the war, Seaborg and others would file hundreds of initially classifed <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patents</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inside_the_Atomic_Patent_Office_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Inside_the_Atomic_Patent_Office-18">[18]</a></sup> most notably <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Wigner" title="Eugene Wigner">Eugene Wigner</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alvin_Weinberg" title="Alvin Weinberg">Alvin Weinberg</a>\'s Patent #2,736,696, on a conceptual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" title="Light water reactor">light water reactor</a> (LWR) that would later become the United States\' primary reactor for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_propulsion" title="Naval propulsion">naval propulsion</a> and later take up the greatest share of the commercial fission-electric landscape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The United Kingdom, Canada,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and the USSR proceeded to research and develop nuclear energy over the course of the late 1940s and early 1950s.\n</p><p>Electricity was generated for the first time by a nuclear reactor on December 20, 1951, at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EBR-I" title="EBR-I">EBR-I</a> experimental station near <a href="/wiki/Arco,_Idaho" title="Arco, Idaho">Arco, Idaho</a>, which initially produced about 100&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KW" title="KW">kW</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\nIn 1953, American President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a> gave his "<a href="/wiki/Atoms_for_Peace" title="Atoms for Peace">Atoms for Peace</a>" speech at the United Nations, emphasizing the need to develop "peaceful" uses of nuclear power quickly. This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Energy_Act_of_1954" title="Atomic Energy Act of 1954">1954 Amendments to the Atomic Energy Act</a> which allowed rapid declassification of U.S. reactor technology and encouraged development by the private sector.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nautiluscore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="386" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nautiluscore.jpg/200px-Nautiluscore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nautiluscore.jpg/300px-Nautiluscore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Nautiluscore.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nautiluscore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The launching ceremony of the <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS&#160;</a></i><a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">Nautilus<i></i></a> January 1954. In 1958 it would become the first vessel to reach the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The first organization to develop nuclear power was the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/S1W_reactor" title="S1W reactor">S1W reactor</a> for the purpose of propelling <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a>. The first nuclear-powered submarine, <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS&#160;<i>Nautilus</i></a>, was put to sea in January 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaeapdf_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-iaeapdf-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The trajectory of civil reactor design was heavily influenced by Admiral <a href="/wiki/Hyman_G._Rickover" title="Hyman G. Rickover">Hyman G. Rickover</a>, who with Weinberg as a close advisor, selected the PWR/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressurized_Water_Reactor" title="Pressurized Water Reactor">Pressurized Water Reactor</a> design, in the form of a  <a href="/wiki/S2W_reactor" title="S2W reactor">10 MW reactor</a> for the Nautilus, a decision that would result in the PWR receiving a government commitment to develop, an engineering lead that would result in a lasting impact on the civilian electricity market in the years to come.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Nuclear_Propulsion" title="United States Navy Nuclear Propulsion">United States Navy Nuclear Propulsion</a> design and <a href="/wiki/Naval_Reactors_Facility" title="Naval Reactors Facility">operation community</a>, under Rickover\'s style of attentive management retains a continuing record of zero reactor accidents (defined as the uncontrolled release of fission products to the environment resulting from damage to a reactor core).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bowman_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-bowman-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> with the U.S. Navy fleet of nuclear-powered ships, standing at some 80 vessels as of 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Largest_Naval_force_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Largest_Naval_force-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On June 27, 1954, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Obninsk_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Obninsk Nuclear Power Plant">Obninsk Nuclear Power Plant</a>, based on what would become the prototype of the <a href="/wiki/RBMK" title="RBMK">RBMK</a> reactor design, became the world\'s first nuclear power plant to generate electricity for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_grid" title="Power grid">power grid</a>, producing around 5 megawatts of electric power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEANews_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEANews-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On July 17, 1955 the <a href="/wiki/BORAX_experiments" title="BORAX experiments">BORAX III</a> reactor, the prototype to later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boiling_Water_Reactor" title="Boiling Water Reactor">Boiling Water Reactors</a>, became the first to generate electricity for an entire community, the town of <a href="/wiki/Arco,_Idaho" title="Arco, Idaho">Arco, Idaho</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> A motion picture record of the demonstration, of supplying some 2 megawatts(2 MW) of electricity, was presented to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Where at the "First Geneva Conference",  the world\'s largest gathering of scientists and engineers, met to explore the technology in that year. In 1957 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EURATOM" title="EURATOM">EURATOM</a> was launched alongside the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (the latter is now the European Union). The same year also saw the launch of the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a> (IAEA).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_(11823864155).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2558" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/220px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/330px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/440px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_(11823864155).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station" title="Calder Hall nuclear power station">Calder Hall nuclear power station</a> in the United Kingdom was the world\'s first commercial nuclear power station. It was connected to the national power grid on 27 August 1956 and officially revealed in a ceremony by Queen Elizabeth II on 17 October 1956. In common with a number of other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generation_I_reactor" title="Generation I reactor">Generation I nuclear reactors</a>, the plant had the dual purpose of producing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_power" title="Electrical power">electrical power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a>, the latter for the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom">nascent nuclear weapons program in Britain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shippingport_Reactor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="516" data-file-width="662" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shippingport_Reactor.jpg/220px-Shippingport_Reactor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shippingport_Reactor.jpg/330px-Shippingport_Reactor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shippingport_Reactor.jpg/440px-Shippingport_Reactor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shippingport_Reactor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 60 MWe <a href="/wiki/Shippingport_Atomic_Power_Station" title="Shippingport Atomic Power Station">Shippingport Atomic Power Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, opened in 1957 and  originating from a cancelled <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">nuclear-powered</a> <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a> contract<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> the Pressurized water reactor design became the first commercial reactor in the United States and the first devoted exclusively to peacetime uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Early_adopter" title="Early adopter">early adoption</a>, a case of technological lock-in,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> and familiarity amongst retired naval personnel, established the PWR as the predominant civilian reactor design, that it still retains today in the US.</div></div></div>\n<p>The world\'s first "commercial nuclear power station", <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station" title="Calder Hall nuclear power station">Calder Hall</a> at Windscale, England, was opened in 1956 with an initial capacity of 50 MW per reactor (200 MW total),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kragh_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kragh-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc17oct_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc17oct-42">[42]</a></sup> it was the first of a fleet of dual-purpose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MAGNOX" title="MAGNOX">MAGNOX</a> reactors, though officially code-named PIPPA(Pressurized Pile Producing Power and Plutonium) by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UKAEA" title="UKAEA">UKAEA</a> to denote the plant\'s dual commercial and military role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army</a>, <a href="/wiki/Army_Nuclear_Power_Program" title="Army Nuclear Power Program">nuclear power program</a>, formally commenced in 1954. Under its management, the 2 megawatt <a href="/wiki/SM-1" title="SM-1">SM-1</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Belvoir" title="Fort Belvoir">Fort Belvoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, was the first in the United States to supply electricity in an industrial capacity to the commercial grid (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VEPCO" title="VEPCO">VEPCO</a>), in April 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first commercial nuclear station to become operational in the United States was the 60 MW <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shippingport_Reactor" title="Shippingport Reactor">Shippingport Reactor</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, in December 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 3 MW <a href="/wiki/SL-1" title="SL-1">SL-1</a> was a U.S. Army experimental nuclear power reactor at the <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory" title="Idaho National Laboratory">National Reactor Testing Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Idaho" title="Eastern Idaho">eastern Idaho</a>, derived from the Borax Boiling water reactor(BWR) design, it first achieved operational <a href="/wiki/Criticality_(status)" title="Criticality (status)">criticality</a>/connection to the grid in 1958. For reasons unknown, in 1961 a technician removed a control rod about 22 inches farther than the prescribed 4 inches. This resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Steam_explosion" title="Steam explosion">steam explosion</a> which killed the three crew members and caused a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_meltdown" title="Nuclear meltdown">meltdown</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ido19313_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-ido19313-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The event was eventually rated at 4 on the seven-level <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/INES_scale" title="INES scale">INES scale</a>.\n</p><p>In service from 1963 and operated as the experimental testbed for the later <a href="/wiki/Alfa-class_submarine" title="Alfa-class submarine">Alfa-class submarine</a> fleet, one of the two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid-metal-cooled_reactor" title="Liquid-metal-cooled reactor">liquid metal cooled</a> reactors onboard the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-27" title="Soviet submarine K-27">Soviet submarine&#160;<i>K-27</i></a>, underwent a <a href="/wiki/Fuel_element_failure" title="Fuel element failure">Fuel element failure</a> accident in 1968, with the emission of gaseous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission products</a> into the surrounding air, producing 9 crew fatalities and 83 injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnston2007_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-johnston2007-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_and_early_opposition_to_nuclear_power">Development and early opposition to nuclear power</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_history.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nuclear_power_history.svg/220px-Nuclear_power_history.svg.png" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_history.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Number of generating and under construction civilian fission-electric reactors, over the period 1960 to 2015.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">Generation II reactor</a> and <a style="width:100%;list-style:none;-webkit-column-width:12em;-moz-column-width:12em;column-width:12em;"><li><span style="padding:0 1em;background-color:red;border:1px solid red;margin-right:1em;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;">&#160;</span> PWR: 277 (63.2%)</li>\n<li><span style="padding:0 1em;background-color:blue;border:1px solid blue;margin-right:1em;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;">&#160;</span> BWR: 80 (18.3%)</li>\n<li><span style="padding:0 1em;background-color:green;border:1px solid green;margin-right:1em;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;">&#160;</span> GCR: 15 (3.4%)</li>\n<li><span style="padding:0 1em;background-color:yellow;border:1px solid yellow;margin-right:1em;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;">&#160;</span> PHWR: 49 (11.2%)</li>\n<li><span style="padding:0 1em;background-color:fuchsia;border:1px solid fuchsia;margin-right:1em;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;">&#160;</span> LWGR: 15 (3.4%)</li>\n<li><span style="padding:0 1em;background-color:aqua;border:1px solid aqua;margin-right:1em;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;">&#160;</span> FBR: 2 (0.5%)</li></ul>\n</div>\n</div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Number of electricity generating civilian reactors by type (end 2014): 277 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressurized_Water_Reactor" title="Pressurized Water Reactor">Pressurized Water Reactors</a>, 80 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boiling_Water_Reactor" title="Boiling Water Reactor">Boiling Water Reactors</a>, 15 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_Cooled_Reactor" title="Gas Cooled Reactor">Gas Cooled Reactors</a>, 49 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressurized_Heavy_Water_Reactor" title="Pressurized Heavy Water Reactor">Pressurized Heavy Water Reactors</a> (<a href="/wiki/CANDU_reactor" title="CANDU reactor">CANDU</a>), 15 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LWGR" title="LWGR">LWGR</a> (RBMK), and 2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_Breeder_Reactor" title="Fast Breeder Reactor">Fast Breeder Reactors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEA_reactors_stats_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA_reactors_stats-49">[49]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The total global installed nuclear capacity initially rose relatively quickly, rising from less than 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">gigawatt</a> (GW) in 1960 to 100 GW in the late 1970s, and 300 GW in the late 1980s. Since the late 1980s worldwide capacity has risen much more slowly, reaching 366 GW in 2005. Between around 1970 and 1990, more than 50 GW of capacity was under construction (peaking at over 150 GW in the late 1970s and early 1980s)&#8212;in 2005, around 25 GW of new capacity was planned. More than two-thirds of all nuclear plants ordered after January 1970 were eventually cancelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaeapdf_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-iaeapdf-28">[28]</a></sup> A total of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_canceled_nuclear_plants_in_the_United_States" title="List of canceled nuclear plants in the United States">63 nuclear units were canceled</a> in the United States between 1975 and 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1972 Alvin Weinberg, co-inventor of the light water reactor design (the most common nuclear reactors today) was fired from his job at <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nixon_administration" title="Nixon administration">Nixon administration</a>, "at least in part" over his raising of concerns about the safety and wisdom of ever larger scaling-up of his design, especially above a power rating of ~500 MW<sub>e</sub>, as in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loss_of_coolant_accident" title="Loss of coolant accident">loss of coolant accident</a> scenario, the <a href="/wiki/Decay_heat" title="Decay heat">decay heat</a> generated from such large compact solid-fuel cores was thought to be beyond the capabilities of passive/natural <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> cooling to prevent a rapid fuel rod melt-down and resulting in then, potential far reaching <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission product</a> pluming. While considering the LWR, well suited at sea for the submarine and naval fleet, Weinberg did not show complete support for its use by utilities on land at the power output that they were interested in for <a href="/wiki/Supply_(economics)" title="Supply (economics)">supply scale</a> reasons, and would request for a greater share of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">AEC</a> research funding to evolve his team\'s demonstrated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Molten-Salt_Reactor_Experiment" title="Molten-Salt Reactor Experiment">Molten-Salt Reactor Experiment</a>, a design with greater inherent safety in this scenario and with that an envisioned greater economic growth potential in the market of large-scale civilian electricity generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Similar to the earlier BORAX reactor safety experiments, conducted by <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> in 1976 <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory" title="Idaho National Laboratory">Idaho National Laboratory</a> began a <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory#Loss_of_Fluid_Test_(LOFT)_facility" title="Idaho National Laboratory">test program focused on LWR reactors</a> under various accident scenarios, with the aim of understanding the event progression and mitigating steps necessary to respond to a failure of one or more of the disparate systems, with much of the redundant back-up safety equipment and nuclear regulations drawing from these series of <a href="/wiki/Destructive_testing" title="Destructive testing">destructive testing</a> investigations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1970s and 1980s rising economic costs (related to extended construction times largely due to regulatory changes and pressure-group litigation)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> and falling fossil fuel prices made nuclear power plants then under construction less attractive. In the 1980s in the U.S. and 1990s in Europe, the flat electric grid growth and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electricity_liberalization" title="Electricity liberalization">electricity liberalization</a> also made the addition of large new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baseload" title="Baseload">baseload</a> energy generators economically unattractive.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electricity_in_France.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Electricity_in_France.svg/220px-Electricity_in_France.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Electricity_in_France.svg/330px-Electricity_in_France.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Electricity_in_France.svg/440px-Electricity_in_France.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electricity_in_France.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Electricity_sector_in_France" title="Electricity sector in France">Electricity production in France</a>, previously dominated by fossil fuels, has been <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_France" title="Nuclear power in France">dominated by nuclear power</a> since the early 1980s, and a large portion of that power is exported to neighboring countries. <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #D55E00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;thermofossil</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0072B2; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;hydroelectric</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #F0E442; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;nuclear</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #009E73; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Other renewables</div></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a> had a significant effect on countries, such as France and Japan, which had relied more heavily on oil for electric generation (39%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and 73% respectively) to invest in nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\nThe French plan, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Messmer_plan" title="Messmer plan">Messmer plan</a>, was for the complete independence from oil, with an envisaged construction of 80 reactors by 1985 and 170 by 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cahiers_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-cahiers-60">[60]</a></sup>\nFrance would construct <a href="/wiki/List_of_power_stations_in_France" title="List of power stations in France">25 fission-electric stations</a>, installing 56 mostly PWR design reactors over the next 15 years, though foregoing the 100 reactors initially charted in 1973, for the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palfreman_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-palfreman-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_preneuf_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_preneuf-62">[62]</a></sup> In 2017, 72% of French electricity was generated by 58 reactors, the highest percentage by any nation in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some local <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_the_United_States#Opposition_to_nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power in the United States">opposition to nuclear power emerged in the U.S.</a> in the early 1960s, beginning with the proposed <a href="/wiki/Bodega_Bay" title="Bodega Bay">Bodega Bay</a> station in California, in 1958, which produced conflict with local citizens and by 1964 the concept was ultimately abandoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-well_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-well-64">[64]</a></sup> In the late 1960s some members of the scientific community began to express pointed concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfgang_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-wolfgang-65">[65]</a></sup> These <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear" title="Anti-nuclear">anti-nuclear</a> concerns related to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents and incidents">nuclear accidents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">nuclear terrorism</a> and <a href="/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste_management" title="High-level radioactive waste management">radioactive waste disposal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bm_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-bm-66">[66]</a></sup> In the early 1970s, there were large protests about a proposed nuclear power plant in <a href="/wiki/Wyhl" title="Wyhl">Wyhl</a>, Germany. The project was cancelled in 1975 the anti-nuclear success at Wyhl inspired opposition to nuclear power in other parts of Europe and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pub_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-pub-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-got_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-got-68">[68]</a></sup> By the mid-1970s anti-nuclear activism gained a wider appeal and influence, and nuclear power began to become an issue of major public protest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jimfalk_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-jimfalk-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eleven_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-eleven-70">[70]</a></sup> In some countries, the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">nuclear power conflict</a> "reached an intensity unprecedented in the history of technology controversies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marcuse.org_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-marcuse.org-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kits_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-kits-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kits_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-kits-72">[72]</a></sup> In May 1979, an estimated 70,000 people, including then governor of California <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Brown" title="Jerry Brown">Jerry Brown</a>, attended a march against nuclear power in Washington, D.C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diablo_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-diablo-73">[73]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_anti-nuclear_power_groups" title="List of anti-nuclear power groups">Anti-nuclear power groups</a> emerged in every country that had a nuclear power programme.\n</p><p>Globally during the 1980s one new nuclear reactor started up every 17&#160;days on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Regulations.2C_pricing_and_accidents"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulations,_pricing_and_accidents">Regulations, pricing and accidents</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl-LWR-comparison.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="718" data-file-width="1181" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Chernobyl-LWR-comparison.PNG/220px-Chernobyl-LWR-comparison.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Chernobyl-LWR-comparison.PNG/330px-Chernobyl-LWR-comparison.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Chernobyl-LWR-comparison.PNG/440px-Chernobyl-LWR-comparison.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chernobyl-LWR-comparison.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A simplified diagram of the major differences between the most common nuclear reactor design, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" title="Light water reactor">Light water reactor</a> and the RBMK (Chernobyl) design. 1. In "red", the use of a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">graphite moderator</a> in a water cooled reactor. 2. A positive steam <a href="/wiki/Void_coefficient" title="Void coefficient">void coefficient</a> that made the power excursion possible, which blew the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactor_vessel" title="Reactor vessel">reactor vessel</a>. 3. The control rods were very slow, taking 18&#8211;20 seconds to be deployed. With the control rods having <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> tips that <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">moderated</a> and therefore increased the fission rate in the beginning of the rod insertion. 4. No reinforced <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment building</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-insag7_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-insag7-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECD02-Ch2_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-OECD02-Ch2-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Starting in the early 1970s in the U.S. and occurring within a atmosphere of increased public hostility, involving the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission#Public_opinion_and_abolishment_of_the_AEC" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">public opposition to the AEC</a> and the eventual founding of its replacement, the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a>, both had attempted to respond to public opinion by lengthening the license procurement process, tightening engineering regulations and increasing the requirements for safety equipment in what is considered the beginning of the regulatory-ratcheting phase of commercial development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Together with relatively minor percentage increases in the total quantity of steel, piping, cabling and concrete per unit of installed <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a>, the more notable changes to the regulatory open <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_hearing" title="Public hearing">public hearing</a>-response cycle for the granting of construction licenses, had the effect of what was once an initial 16 months for project initiation to the pouring of first concrete in 1967, escalating to 32 months in 1972 and finally 54 months in 1980, which ultimately, quadrupled the price of power reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Utility proposals in the U.S for nuclear generating stations, peaked at 52 in 1974, fell to 12 in 1976 and have never recovered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> in large part due to the pressure-group litigation strategy, of launching lawsuits against each proposed U.S construction proposal,  keeping private utilities tied up in court for years, one of which having reached <a href="/wiki/Vermont_Yankee_Nuclear_Power_Corp._v._Natural_Resources_Defense_Council,_Inc." title="Vermont Yankee Nuclear Power Corp. v. Natural Resources Defense Council, Inc.">the supreme court in 1978</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> With permission to build a nuclear station in the U.S. eventually taking longer than in any other industrial country, the spectre facing utilities of having to pay interest on large construction loans while the anti-nuclear movement used the legal system to produce delays, increasingly made the viablity of financing construction, less certain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> By the close of the 1970s it became clear that nuclear power would not grow nearly as dramatically as once believed.\n</p><p>Over <a href="/wiki/List_of_cancelled_nuclear_reactors_in_the_United_States" title="List of cancelled nuclear reactors in the United States">120 reactor proposals in the United States</a> were ultimately cancelled<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and the construction of new reactors ground to a halt. A cover story in the February 11, 1985, issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> magazine commented on the overall failure of the U.S. nuclear power program, saying it "ranks as the largest managerial disaster in business history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to some commentators, the 1979 <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">accident at Three Mile Island</a> (TMI) played a major part in the reduction in the number of new plant constructions in many other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfgang_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-wolfgang-65">[65]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">NRC</a>, TMI was the most serious accident in "U.S. commercial nuclear power plant operating history, even though it led to no deaths or injuries to plant workers or members of the nearby community."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The regulatory uncertainty and delays eventually resulted in an escalation of construction related debt that led to the bankruptcy of Seabrook\'s major utility owner, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_Service_Company_of_New_Hampshire" title="Public Service Company of New Hampshire">Public Service Company of New Hampshire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> At the time, the fourth <a href="/wiki/Chapter_11,_Title_11,_United_States_Code#Largest_bankruptcies" title="Chapter 11, Title 11, United States Code">largest bankruptcy</a> in United States corporate history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Amongst US engineers, the cost increases from implementing the regulatory changes that resulted from the TMI accident were, when eventually finalized, only a few percent of total construction costs for new reactors, primarily relating to the prevention of safety systems from being turned off. With the most significant engineering result of the TMI accident, the recognition that better operator training was needed and that the existing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_core_cooling_system" title="Emergency core cooling system">emergency core cooling system</a> of PWRs worked better in a real-world emergency than members of the anti-nuclear movement had routinely claimed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2157" data-file-width="3835" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg/220px-%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg/330px-%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg/440px-%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0_%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BF%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%84%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5_4_%D1%8D%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%B0_%D0%A7%D0%90%D0%AD%D0%A1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The abandoned town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pripyat_(city)" title="Pripyat (city)">Pripyat</a> since 1986, with the Chernobyl plant and the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_New_Safe_Confinement" title="Chernobyl New Safe Confinement">Chernobyl New Safe Confinement</a> arch in the distance, prior to it moving into place and retaining the hazardous dust generated during the disassembly process, on site.</div></div></div>\n<p>The already slowing rate of new construction along with the shutdown in the 1980s of two existing demonstration nuclear power stations in the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Valley" title="Tennessee Valley">Tennessee Valley</a>, US, when they couldn&#8217;t economically meet the NRC\'s new tightened standards, shifted electricity generation to coal-fired power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> In 1977, following the first oil shock, U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> made a speech calling the energy crisis the "<a href="/wiki/Moral_Equivalent_of_War_speech" title="Moral Equivalent of War speech">moral equivalent of war</a>" and prominently supporting nuclear power.  However, nuclear power could not compete with cheap oil and gas, particularly after public opposition and regulatory hurdles made new nuclear prohibitively expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2006 The <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a>, a public policy organization, stated that new nuclear units had not been built in the United States because of soft demand for electricity, the potential <a href="/wiki/Cost_overrun" title="Cost overrun">cost overruns</a> on nuclear reactors due to regulatory issues and resulting construction delays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tbi_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-tbi-94">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1982, amongst a backdrop of ongoing protests directed at the construction of the first commercial scale <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">breeder reactor</a> in France, a later member of the <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_Switzerland" title="Green Party of Switzerland">Swiss Green Party</a> fired five <a href="/wiki/RPG-7" title="RPG-7">RPG-7</a> <a href="/wiki/Rocket-propelled_grenade" title="Rocket-propelled grenade">rocket-propelled grenades</a> at the still under construction <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment building</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superphenix" title="Superphenix">Superphenix</a> reactor. Two grenades hit and caused minor damage to the reinforced concrete outer shell. It was the first time protests reached such heights. After examination of the superficial damage, the prototype fast breeder reactor started and operated for over a decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to some commentators, the 1986 <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a> played a major part in the reduction in the number of new plant constructions in many other countries:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfgang_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-wolfgang-65">[65]</a></sup>\nUnlike the Three Mile Island accident the much more serious Chernobyl accident did not increase regulations or engineering changes affecting Western reactors; because the <a href="/wiki/RBMK" title="RBMK">RBMK</a> design, which lacks safety features such as "robust" <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment buildings</a>, was only used in the Soviet Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRC-96">[96]</a></sup> Over 10 RBMK reactors are still in use today. However, changes were made in both the RBMK reactors themselves (use of a safer enrichment of uranium) and in the control system (preventing safety systems being disabled), amongst other things, to reduce the possibility of a similar accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>  Russia now largely relies upon, builds and exports a variant of the PWR, the <a href="/wiki/VVER" title="VVER">VVER</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Russia" title="Nuclear power in Russia">over 20 in use today</a>.\n</p><p>An international organization to promote safety awareness and the professional development of operators in nuclear facilities, the <a href="/wiki/World_Association_of_Nuclear_Operators" title="World Association of Nuclear Operators">World Association of Nuclear Operators</a> (WANO), was created as a direct outcome of the 1986 Chernobyl accident. The organization was created with the intent to share and grow the adoption of nuclear safety culture, technology and community, where before there was an atmosphere of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold_war" title="Cold war">cold war</a> secrecy.\n</p><p>Numerous countries, including Austria (1978), Sweden (1980) and Italy (1987) (influenced by Chernobyl) have voted in referendums to oppose or phase out nuclear power.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_renaissance">Nuclear renaissance</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OL3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1320" data-file-width="2813" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/250px-OL3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/375px-OL3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/500px-OL3.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OL3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olkiluoto_3" title="Olkiluoto 3">Olkiluoto 3</a> under construction in 2009. It was the first <a href="/wiki/EPR_(nuclear_reactor)" title="EPR (nuclear reactor)">EPR</a>, a modernized PWR design with a <a href="/wiki/Core_catcher" title="Core catcher">core catcher</a>, to start construction but problems with workmanship and supervision have created costly delays which led to an inquiry by the Finnish nuclear regulator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C3%A4teilyturvakeskus" title="S&#228;teilyturvakeskus">STUK</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wnn161009_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-wnn161009-98">[98]</a></sup> Allowing the later started <a href="/wiki/Taishan_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Taishan Nuclear Power Plant">Taishan Nuclear EPR project</a> to reach first connection to a national grid, in 2018. In December 2012, Areva estimated that the full cost of Olkiluoto will be about &#8364;8.5 billion, or almost three times the original delivery price of &#8364;3 billion and it will not be connected to the grid until the 2020s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNN-2012-07-17_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNN-2012-07-17-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-2012-07-16_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-2012-07-16-101">[101]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div
