style="position:absolute;left:175.5px;height:10px;width:1px;border-left:1px solid black;"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Operational nuclear reactors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRISstatsglobal_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-PRISstatsglobal-102">[102]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_renaissance" title="Nuclear renaissance">Nuclear renaissance</a></div>\n<p>With the energy source, as of 2010, an established alternative, providing two thirds(2/3) of the twenty seven nation <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-carbon_energy" title="Low-carbon energy">low-carbon electricity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>\nIn 2009, Petteri Tiippana, the director of STUK\'s nuclear power plant division, told the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> that it was difficult to deliver a <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">Generation III reactor</a> project on schedule because builders were not used to working to the exacting standards required on nuclear construction sites, since so few new reactors had been built in recent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2018 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a> Energy Initiative study on the future of nuclear energy concluded that together with the strong suggestion that government should financially support development and demonstration of new Generation IV nuclear technologies, for a worldwide renaissance to commence, a global standardization of regulations needs to take place, with a move towards serial manufacturing of standardized units akin to the other complex engineering field of aircraft and aviation. At present it is common for each country to demand <a href="/wiki/Bespoke" title="Bespoke">bespoke</a> changes to the design to satisfy varying national regulatory bodies, often to the benefit of domestic engineering supply firms. The report goes on to note that the most cost-effective projects have been built with multiple (up to six) reactors per site using a standardized design, with the same component suppliers and construction crews working on each unit, in a continuous work flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Disaster">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Disaster</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Disaster">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Disaster</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant</a></div>\n<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 T&#333;hoku earthquake and tsunami">T&#333;hoku earthquake</a> on 11 March 2011, one of the largest earthquakes ever recorded, and a subsequent tsunami off the coast of Japan, the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant</a> suffered multiple core meltdowns due to failure of the emergency cooling system for lack of electricity supply. This resulted in the most serious nuclear accident since the Chernobyl disaster.\n</p><p>The Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident prompted a re-examination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" title="Nuclear safety">nuclear safety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">nuclear energy policy</a> in many countries<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciamer2011_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciamer2011-106">[106]</a></sup> and raised questions among some commentators over the future of the renaissance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>\nGermany approved plans to close all its reactors by 2022, Italian nuclear energy plans in 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> ended when Italy banned the generation, but not consumption of, nuclear electricity in a June 2011 referendum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciamer2011_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciamer2011-106">[106]</a></sup>\nChina, Switzerland, Israel, Malaysia, Thailand, United Kingdom, and the Philippines reviewed their nuclear power programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-piersmorgan.blogs.cnn.com_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-piersmorgan.blogs.cnn.com-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.xinhuanet.com_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-news.xinhuanet.com-114">[114]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2011 the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> halved its prior estimate of new generating capacity to be built by 2035.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist-20110428_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-economist-20110428-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-late_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-late-116">[116]</a></sup>\nNuclear power generation had the biggest ever fall year-on-year in 2012, with nuclear power plants globally producing 2,346 TWh of electricity, a drop of 7% from 2011.\nThis was caused primarily by the majority of Japanese reactors remaining offline that year and the permanent closure of eight reactors in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna13_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna13-117">[117]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Fukushima">Post-Fukushima</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster#Equipment,_facility,_and_operational_changes" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster &#167;&#160;Equipment, facility, and operational changes</a></div>\n<p>The Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident sparked controversy about the importance of the accident and its effect on nuclear\'s future.\nThe crisis prompted countries with nuclear power to review the safety of their reactor fleet and reconsider the speed and scale of planned nuclear expansions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platts_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Platts-118">[118]</a></sup>\nIn 2011, <i>The Economist</i> opined that nuclear power "looks dangerous, unpopular, expensive and risky", and suggested a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_phase-out" title="Nuclear phase-out">nuclear phase-out</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ2011_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-econ2011-119">[119]</a></sup>\nJeffrey Sachs, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_Institute" title="Earth Institute">Earth Institute</a> Director, disagreed claiming combating climate change would require an expansion of nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>\nInvestment banks were also critical of nuclear soon after the accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2011 German engineering giant <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> said it would withdraw entirely from the nuclear industry in response to the Fukushima accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC18Sep2011_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC18Sep2011-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In 2017, Siemens set the "milestone" of supplying the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Additive_manufacturing" title="Additive manufacturing">additive manufacturing</a> part to a nuclear power station, at the <a href="/wiki/Kr%C5%A1ko_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Kr&#353;ko Nuclear Power Plant">Kr&#353;ko Nuclear Power Plant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>, which it regards as an "industry breakthrough".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a> reported in 2011 the suggestion that the safety and survival of the younger <a href="/wiki/Onagawa_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Onagawa Nuclear Power Plant">Onagawa Nuclear Power Plant</a>, the closest reactor facility to the <a href="/wiki/Epicenter" title="Epicenter">epicenter</a> and on the coast, demonstrate that it is possible for nuclear facilities to withstand the greatest natural disasters. The Onagawa plant was also said to show that nuclear power can retain public trust, with the surviving residents of the town of Onagawa taking refuge in the gymnasium of the nuclear facility following the destruction of their town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAEA" title="IAEA">IAEA</a> inspection in 2012, the agency stated that "The structural elements of the [Onagawa] NPS (nuclear power station) were remarkably undamaged given the magnitude of ground motion experienced and the duration and size of this great earthquake,&#8221;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 2012, the U.S. NRC approved the construction of 2 reactors at the <a href="/wiki/Vogtle_Electric_Generating_Plant" title="Vogtle Electric Generating Plant">Vogtle Electric Generating Plant</a>, the first approval in 30 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yah12_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-yah12-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In August 2015, following 4 years of near zero fission-electricity generation, Japan began restarting its nuclear reactors, after <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster#Equipment,_facility,_and_operational_changes" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">safety upgrades were completed</a>, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Sendai_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Sendai Nuclear Power Plant">Sendai Nuclear Power Plant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 2015, the IAEA\'s outlook for nuclear energy had become more promising.\n"Nuclear power is a critical element in limiting greenhouse gas emissions," the agency noted, and "the prospects for nuclear energy remain positive in the medium to long term despite a negative impact in some countries in the aftermath of the [Fukushima-Daiichi] accident...it is still the second-largest source worldwide of low-carbon electricity.\nAnd the 72 reactors under construction at the start of last year were the most in 25 years."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>\nAs of 2015 the global trend was for new nuclear power stations coming online to be balanced by the number of old plants being retired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Eight new grid connections were completed by China in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNN20160307_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNN20160307-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_of_development">Future of development</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">Nuclear energy policy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" title="Mitigation of global warming">Mitigation of global warming</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ulchin_(now_Hanul)_04790182_(8506930230).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="3216" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg/220px-Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg/330px-Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg/440px-Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ulchin_(now_Hanul)_04790182_(8506930230).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Hanul_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Hanul Nuclear Power Plant">Hanul Nuclear Power Plant</a> in South Korea, presently the second largest in the world by output, with six operating power reactors. Two additional indigenously designed <a href="/wiki/APR-1400" title="APR-1400">APR-1400</a> generation-III reactors are under construction. Korea exported the APR design to the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, were four of these reactors are under construction at <a href="/wiki/Barakah_nuclear_power_plant" title="Barakah nuclear power plant">Barakah nuclear power plant</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>As of 2018, there are over 150 nuclear reactors planned including 50 under construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> However, while investment on upgrades of existing plant and life-time extensions continues,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wnn-20180717_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-wnn-20180717-138">[138]</a></sup> investment in new nuclear is declining, reaching a 5-year-low in 2017.\n</p><p>In 2016, the U.S. Energy Information Administration projected for its &#8220;base case&#8221; that world nuclear power generation would increase from 2,344 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatt_hour" title="Terawatt hour">terawatt hours</a> (TWh) in 2012 to 4,500 TWh in 2040.\nMost of the predicted increase was expected to be in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The future of nuclear power varies greatly between countries, depending on government policies.\nSome countries, most notably, Germany, have adopted policies of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_phase-out" title="Nuclear power phase-out">nuclear power phase-out</a>.\nAt the same time, some Asian countries, such as China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-china19_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-china19-140">[140]</a></sup> and India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> have committed to rapid expansion of nuclear power.\nMany other countries, such as the United Kingdom<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> and the United States, have policies in between.\nJapan generated about 30% of its electricity from nuclear power before the Fukushima accident.\nIn 2015 the Japanese government committed to the aim of restarting its fleet of 40 reactors by 2030 after safety upgrades, and to finish the construction of the Generation III <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Cma_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="&#332;ma Nuclear Power Plant">&#332;ma Nuclear Power Plant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>\nThis would mean that approximately 20% of electricity would come from nuclear power by 2030.\nAs of 2018, some reactors have restarted commercial operation following inspections and upgrades with new regulations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>\nWhile <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_South_Korea" title="Nuclear power in South Korea">South Korea has a large nuclear power industry</a>, the new government in 2017,  influenced by a vocal anti-nuclear movement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> committed to halting nuclear development after the completion of the facilities presently under construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nei-20180130_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-nei-20180130-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wnn-20170619_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-wnn-20170619-147">[147]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:435px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GenIVRoadmap-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="971" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/GenIVRoadmap-en.svg/433px-GenIVRoadmap-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/GenIVRoadmap-en.svg/650px-GenIVRoadmap-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/GenIVRoadmap-en.svg/866px-GenIVRoadmap-en.svg.png 2x" width="433"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GenIVRoadmap-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generation_IV" title="Generation IV">Generation IV</a> roadmap. Nuclear Energy Systems Deployable no later than 2030 and offering significant advances in sustainability, safety and reliability, and economics.</div></div></div>\n<p>The nuclear power industry in some western nations have a history of construction delays, cost overruns, plant cancellations, and nuclear safety issues, despite significant government subsidies and support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jk_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-jk-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greeninc_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-greeninc-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rb_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-rb-150">[150]</a></sup>\nThese problems are related to very strict safety requirements, uncertain regulatory environment, slow rate of construction, and large stretches of time with no nuclear construction and consequent loss of know-how.\nCommentators therefore argue that new nuclear is impractical in western countries because of popular opposition, regulatory uncertainty, soft demand for multiple reactor units and high costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Quiggin_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Quiggin-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hannah_Northey_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hannah_Northey-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lowenuke_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-lowenuke-153">[153]</a></sup>\nThe bankruptcy of <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Company" title="Westinghouse Electric Company">Westinghouse</a> in March 2017 due to US$9 billion of losses from the halting of construction at <a href="/wiki/Virgil_C._Summer_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Virgil C. Summer Nuclear Generating Station">Virgil C. Summer Nuclear Generating Station</a>, in the U.S. is considered an advantage for eastern companies, for the future export and design of nuclear fuel and reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nei-20170329_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-nei-20170329-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bl-20170330_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-bl-20170330-155">[155]</a></sup> In 2016, Greenpeace and the wind industry company <a href="/wiki/Ecotricity" title="Ecotricity">Ecotricity</a> criticized the high cost of the <a href="/wiki/Hinkley_Point_C_nuclear_power_station" title="Hinkley Point C nuclear power station">Hinkley Point C nuclear power station</a> and threatened to take action in British or French courts or lodge a complaint with the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>, in order to trigger an investigation, which they said could last as long as a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The greatest new build activity is occurring in Asian countries like South Korea, India and China.\nIn January 2019, China had 45 reactors in operation, 13 under construction, and plans to build 43 more, which would make it the world\'s largest generator of nuclear electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-china19_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-china19-140">[140]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3668" data-file-width="2791" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg/200px-Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg/300px-Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg/400px-Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blue light from <a href="/wiki/Cherenkov_radiation" title="Cherenkov radiation">Cherenkov radiation</a> being produced near the core of the Fission powered <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Test_Reactor" title="Advanced Test Reactor">Advanced Test Reactor</a>. A facility taking part in the <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Fuel_Cycle_Initiative" title="Advanced Fuel Cycle Initiative">Advanced Fuel Cycle Initiative</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmute</a> certain <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> into fuel, that would be able to be used in commercial light water reactors, reducing a number of the security hazards of, what is all presently considered "<a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">waste</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATR_Research_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATR_Research-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 2016 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BN-800" title="BN-800">BN-800</a> sodium cooled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_reactor" title="Fast reactor">fast reactor</a> in Russia, began commercial electricity generation, while plans for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BN-1200" title="BN-1200">BN-1200</a> were initially conceived the future of the fast reactor program in Russia awaits the results from MBIR, an under construction multi-loop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generation_IV" title="Generation IV">Generation IV</a> research facility for testing the chemically more inert lead, lead-<a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_cooled_fast_reactor" title="Gas cooled fast reactor">gas coolants</a>, it will similarly run on recycled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOX" title="MOX">MOX</a> (mixed uranium and plutonium oxide) fuel. An on-site <a href="/wiki/Pyroprocessing" title="Pyroprocessing">pyrochemical processing</a>, closed fuel-cycle facility, is planned, to recycle the spent fuel/"waste" and reduce the necessity for a growth in uranium mining and exploration. In 2017 the manufacture program for the reactor commenced with the facility open to collaboration under the "International Project on Innovative Nuclear Reactors and Fuel Cycle", it has a construction schedule, that includes an operational start in 2020. As planned, it will be the world\'s most-powerful research reactor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the United States, licenses of almost half of the operating nuclear reactors have been extended to 60&#160;years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-matt2010_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-matt2010-160">[160]</a></sup>\nThe U.S. NRC and the U.S. Department of Energy have initiated research into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor_sustainability" title="Light water reactor sustainability">Light water reactor sustainability</a> which is hoped will lead to allowing extensions of reactor licenses beyond 60 years, provided that safety can be maintained, to increase energy security and preserve low-carbon generation sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LifeAfter60_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-LifeAfter60-161">[161]</a></sup>\nResearch into nuclear reactors that can last 100 years, known as <a href="/wiki/Centurion_Reactor" title="Centurion Reactor">Centurion Reactors</a>, is being conducted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r2_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-r2-162">[162]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_power_station">Nuclear power station</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" data-dir="ltr"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:PWR nuclear power plant animation.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/PWR_nuclear_power_plant_animation.webm/220px-seek%3D2-PWR_nuclear_power_plant_animation.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e3/PWR_nuclear_power_plant_animation.webm" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PWR_nuclear_power_plant_animation.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An animation of a <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">Pressurized water reactor</a> in operation.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_station" title="Nuclear power station">Nuclear power station</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">List of nuclear reactors</a></div>\n<p>Just as many conventional <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power stations</a> generate electricity by harnessing the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> released from burning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>, nuclear power plants convert the energy released from the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> of an atom via <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> that takes place in a nuclear reactor. When a <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> hits the nucleus of a <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> or <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> atom, it can split the nucleus into two smaller nuclei. The reaction is called nuclear fission. The fission reaction releases energy and neutrons. The released neutrons can hit other uranium or plutonium nuclei, causing new fission reactions, which release more energy and more neutrons. This is called a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">chain reaction</a>. The reaction rate is controlled by <a href="/wiki/Control_rod" title="Control rod">control rods</a> that absorb excess neutrons. The controllability of nuclear reactors depends on the fact that a small fraction of neutrons resulting from fission are <a href="/wiki/Delayed_neutron" title="Delayed neutron">delayed</a>. The time delay between the fission and the release of the neutrons slows down changes in reaction rates and gives time for moving the control rods to adjust the reaction rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAnuclearreactorbasics_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNAnuclearreactorbasics-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A fission nuclear power plant is generally composed of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a>, in which the nuclear reactions generating heat take place; a cooling system, which removes the heat from inside the reactor; a <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a>, which transforms the heat in <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_energy" title="Mechanical energy">mechanical energy</a>; an <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">electric generator</a>, which transform the mechanical energy into electrical energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAnuclearreactorbasics_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNAnuclearreactorbasics-163">[163]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle_of_nuclear_fuel">Life cycle of nuclear fuel</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="914" data-file-width="923" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/220px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/330px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/440px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The nuclear fuel cycle begins when uranium is mined, enriched, and manufactured into nuclear fuel, (1) which is delivered to a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plant</a>. After usage in the power plant, the spent fuel is delivered to a reprocessing plant (2) or to a final repository (3) for geological disposition. In <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">reprocessing</a> 95% of spent fuel can potentially be recycled to be returned to usage in a power plant (4).</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">Nuclear fuel cycle</a></div>\n<p>A nuclear reactor is only part of the fuel life-cycle for nuclear power.\nThe process starts with mining (see <i><a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">Uranium mining</a></i>).\nUranium mines are underground, <a href="/wiki/Open-pit_mining" title="Open-pit mining">open-pit</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ_leach" title="In-situ leach">in-situ leach</a> mines.\nThe uranium ore is extracted, usually converted into a stable and compact form such as <a href="/wiki/Yellowcake" title="Yellowcake">yellowcake</a>, and then transported to a processing facility.\nHere, the yellowcake is converted to <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">uranium hexafluoride</a>, which is then generally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium_enrichment" title="Uranium enrichment">enriched</a> using various techniques.\nSome reactor designs can also use natural uranium without enrichment.\nThe enriched uranium, containing more than the natural 0.7% <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a>, is generally used to make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_rod#Common_physical_forms_of_nuclear_fuel" title="Fuel rod">rods</a> of the proper composition and geometry for the particular reactor that the fuel is destined for.\nIn modern light-water reactors the fuel rods will spend about 3 operational cycles (typically 6 years total now) inside the reactor, generally until about 3% of their uranium has been fissioned, then they will be moved to a <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a> where the short lived isotopes generated by fission can decay away.\nAfter about 5 years in a spent fuel pool the spent fuel is radioactively and thermally cool enough to handle, and can be moved to dry storage casks or reprocessed.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conventional_fuel_resources">Conventional fuel resources</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Uranium_market" title="Uranium market">Uranium market</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_development#Nuclear_energy" title="Energy development">Energy development &#8211; Nuclear</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2200" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="587" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg/200px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg/300px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg/400px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proportions of the isotopes <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a> (blue) and uranium-235 (red) found in natural uranium and in <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">enriched uranium</a> for different applications. Light water reactors use 3-5% enriched uranium, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CANDU" title="CANDU">CANDU</a> reactors work with natural uranium.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">Uranium</a> is a fairly common <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> in the Earth\'s crust: it is approximately as common as <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, and is about 40&#160;times more common than silver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>\nUranium is present in trace concentrations in most rocks, dirt, and ocean water, but is generally economically extracted only where it is present in high concentrations.\nAs of 2011 the world\'s known resources of uranium, economically recoverable at the arbitrary price ceiling of US$130/kg, were enough to last for between 70 and 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Red_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Red-168">[168]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The OECD\'s red book of 2011 said that conventional uranium resources had grown by 12.5% since 2008 due to increased exploration, with this increase translating into greater than a century of uranium available if the rate of use were to continue at the 2011 level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 2007, the OECD estimated 670 years of economically recoverable uranium in total conventional resources and <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> ores assuming the then-current use rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Light water reactors make relatively inefficient use of nuclear fuel, mostly fissioning only the very rare uranium-235 isotope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-wmitnfc_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna-wmitnfc-172">[172]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a> can make this waste reusable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-wmitnfc_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-wna-wmitnfc-172">[172]</a></sup> Newer <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">generation III reactors</a> also achieve a more efficient use of the available resources than the <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">generation II reactors</a> which make up the vast majority of reactors worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-wmitnfc_172-2"><a href="#cite_note-wna-wmitnfc-172">[172]</a></sup>  With a pure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_reactor" title="Fast reactor">fast reactor</a> fuel cycle with a burn up of all the Uranium and <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> (which presently make up the most hazardous substances in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" title="Nuclear waste">nuclear waste</a>), there is an estimated 160,000 years worth of Uranium in total conventional resources and phosphate ore at the price of 60&#8211;100 US$/kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Unconventional_fuel_resources">Unconventional fuel resources</span></h4>\n<p>Unconventional uranium resources also exist.\nUranium is naturally present in seawater at a concentration of about 3 <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">micrograms</a> per liter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.ie_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.ie-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gepr.org_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-gepr.org-178">[178]</a></sup> with 4.5 billion tons of uranium considered present in seawater at any time.\nIn 2012 it was estimated that this fuel source could be extracted at 10 times the current price of uranium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2014, with the advances made in the efficiency of seawater uranium extraction, it was suggested that it would be economically competitive to produce fuel for light water reactors from seawater if the process was implemented at large scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>\nUranium extracted on an industrial scale from seawater would constantly be replenished by both river erosion of rocks and the natural process of uranium <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(metallurgy)" title="Leaching (metallurgy)">dissolved</a> from the surface area of the ocean floor, both of which maintain the <a href="/wiki/Solubility_equilibrium" title="Solubility equilibrium">solubility equilibria</a> of seawater concentration at a stable level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gepr.org_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-gepr.org-178">[178]</a></sup>\nSome commentators have argued that this strengthens the case for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">Nuclear power to be considered a renewable energy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">Breeder reactor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy</a></div>\n<p>As opposed to light water reactors which use uranium-235 (0.7% of all natural uranium), fast breeder reactors use uranium-238 (99.3% of all natural uranium) or thorium. A number of fuel cycles and breeder reactor combinations are considered to be sustainable and/or renewable sources of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brundtland_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brundtland-183">[183]</a></sup> In 2006 it was estimated that with seawater extraction, there was likely some five billion years\' worth of uranium-238 for use in breeder reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanford-cohen_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-cohen-184">[184]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Breeder technology has been used in several reactors, but the high cost of reprocessing fuel safely, at 2006 technological levels, requires uranium prices of more than US$200/kg before becoming justified economically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-anpr_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna-anpr-185">[185]</a></sup> Breeder reactors are however being pursued as they have the potential to burn up all of the actinides in the present inventory of nuclear waste while also producing power and creating additional quantities of fuel for more reactors via the breeding process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of 2017, there are two breeders producing commercial power, <a href="/wiki/BN-600_reactor" title="BN-600 reactor">BN-600 reactor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/BN-800_reactor" title="BN-800 reactor">BN-800 reactor</a>, both in Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAfast_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNAfast-188">[188]</a></sup>\nThe BN-600, with a capacity of 600 MW, was built in 1980 in Beloyarsk and is planned to produce power until 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAfast_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNAfast-188">[188]</a></sup> The BN-800 is an updated version of the BN-600, and started operation in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAfast_188-2"><a href="#cite_note-WNAfast-188">[188]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ph%C3%A9nix" title="Ph&#233;nix">Ph&#233;nix</a> breeder reactor in France was powered down in 2009 after 36 years of operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAfast_188-3"><a href="#cite_note-WNAfast-188">[188]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg/220px-RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg/330px-RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg/440px-RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_rod" title="Nuclear fuel rod">nuclear fuel rod</a> assembly bundle being inspected before entering a reactor.</div></div></div>\n<p>Both China and India are building breeder reactors.\nThe Indian 500 MWe <a href="/wiki/Prototype_Fast_Breeder_Reactor" title="Prototype Fast Breeder Reactor">Prototype Fast Breeder Reactor</a> is in the commissioning phase,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> with plans to build more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another alternative to fast breeders is thermal breeder reactors that use uranium-233 bred from <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> as fission fuel in the <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle" title="Thorium fuel cycle">thorium fuel cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-191">[191]</a></sup> Thorium is about 3.5 times more common than uranium in the Earth\'s crust, and has different geographic characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-191">[191]</a></sup> This would extend the total practical fissionable resource base by 450%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_191-2"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-191">[191]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/India%27s_three-stage_nuclear_power_programme" title="India\'s three-stage nuclear power programme">India\'s three-stage nuclear power programme</a> features the use of a thorium fuel cycle in the third stage, as it has abundant thorium reserves but little uranium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_191-3"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-191">[191]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_waste">Nuclear waste</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sievert#Dose_rate_examples" title="Sievert">Sievert &#167;&#160;Dose rate examples</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spent_Fuel_Storage_(25865854820).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2047" data-file-width="2628" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Spent_Fuel_Storage_%2825865854820%29.png/220px-Spent_Fuel_Storage_%2825865854820%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Spent_Fuel_Storage_%2825865854820%29.png/330px-Spent_Fuel_Storage_%2825865854820%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Spent_Fuel_Storage_%2825865854820%29.png/440px-Spent_Fuel_Storage_%2825865854820%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spent_Fuel_Storage_(25865854820).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The lifecycle of fuel in the present US system. If put in one place the total inventory of spent nuclear fuel generated by the commercial fleet of power stations in the United States, would stand 25 feet tall and be 300 feet on a side, approximately the footprint of one <a href="/wiki/American_football#Field_and_equipment" title="American football">football field</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Generation_Atomic_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Generation_Atomic-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr.org_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-npr.org-193">[193]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The most important waste stream from nuclear power reactors is <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a>. From LWRs, it is typically composed of 95% uranium, 4% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission products</a> from the energy generating nuclear fission reactions, as well as about 1% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic" title="Transuranic">transuranic</a> actinides (mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactor_grade_plutonium" title="Reactor grade plutonium">reactor grade plutonium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> from unavoidable <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> events.  The plutonium and other transuranics are responsible for the bulk of the long-term radioactivity, whereas the fission products are responsible for the bulk of the short-term radioactivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High-level_radioactive_waste">High-level radioactive waste</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Reactor-grade_plutonium#Reuse_in_reactors" title="Reactor-grade plutonium">Reactor-grade plutonium &#167;&#160;Reuse in reactors</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_waste_treatment_technologies" title="List of nuclear waste treatment technologies">List of nuclear waste treatment technologies</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="572" data-file-width="972" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg/220px-Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg/330px-Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg/440px-Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fuel_composition.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typical composition of <a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">UOx</a> before and after approximately 3 years of fission service in the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle#Once-through_nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">once-thru fuel cycle</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LWR" title="LWR">LWR</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-web.archive.org_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-web.archive.org-196">[196]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_neutron" title="Thermal neutron">Thermal neutron</a>-spectrum-reactors, which presently constitute the majority of the world fleet, cannot burn up the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactor_grade_plutonium" title="Reactor grade plutonium">reactor grade plutonium</a> that is generated efficiently, limiting the effective useful fuel life to a few years at most. Reactors in Europe and Asia are permitted to burn later refined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOX" title="MOX">MOX</a> fuel, though the <a href="/wiki/Burnup" title="Burnup">burnup</a> is similarly not complete.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="976" data-file-width="1304" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg/220px-Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg/330px-Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg/440px-Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spent_nuclear_fuel_decay_sievert.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the years outside a reactor, the activity of spent UOx fuel, in comparison to the activity of <a href="/wiki/Uraninite" title="Uraninite">natural uranium ore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-web.archive.org_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-web.archive.org-196">[196]</a></sup>  The various <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_plutonium" title="Isotopes of plutonium">plutonium isotopes</a> that are generated and <a href="/wiki/Minor_actinide" title="Minor actinide">minor actinides</a> constitute the primary hazard following the relatively rapid decay of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_products" title="Fission products">fission products</a> after approximately 300 years. The long lived fission products, Tc-99 and I-129, though less radioactive than the natural uranium ore they derived from,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> are the focus of much thought on containing.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1139" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/220px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/330px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/440px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Following interim storage in a <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a>, the bundles of used fuel rod assemblies of a typical nuclear power station are often stored on site in the likes of the eight <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry cask storage</a> vessels pictured above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Rowe_Nuclear_Power_Station" title="Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station">Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station</a>, which generated 44 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hours" title="Kilowatt hours">kilowatt hours</a> of electricity when in service, its complete spent fuel inventory is contained within sixteen casks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>  It is commonly estimated that to produce a per <a href="/wiki/Capita" title="Capita">capita</a> lifetime supply of energy at a western <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a>, approximately 3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GWh" title="GWh">GWh</a>, would require on the order of the volume of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soda_can" title="Soda can">soda can</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_enriched_uranium" title="Low enriched uranium">Low enriched uranium</a> per person and thus result in a similar volume of spent fuel generated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Generation_Atomic_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-Generation_Atomic-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr.org_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-npr.org-193">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The high-level radioactive waste/spent fuel that is generated from power production, requires treatment, management and isolation from the environment. The technical issues in accomplishing this are considerable, due to the extremely long periods some particularly <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimation</a> prone, mildly <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">radioactive wastes</a>, remain potentially hazardous to living organisms, namely the <a href="/wiki/Long-lived_fission_product" title="Long-lived fission product">long-lived fission products</a>, <a href="/wiki/Technetium-99" title="Technetium-99">Technetium-99</a> (half-life 220,000 years) and <a href="/wiki/Iodine-129" title="Iodine-129">Iodine-129</a> (half-life 15.7 million years),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> which dominate the waste stream in radioactivity after the more intensely radioactive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_products" title="Fission products">short-lived fission products</a>(SLFPs)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-web.archive.org_196-2"><a href="#cite_note-web.archive.org-196">[196]</a></sup> have decayed into stable elements, which takes approximately 300 years. To successfully isolate the LLFP waste from the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>, either separation and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation#Long-lived_fission_products_(LLFP)" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-web.archive.org_196-3"><a href="#cite_note-web.archive.org-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> or some variation of a <a href="/wiki/Synroc" title="Synroc">synroc</a> treatment and deep geological storage, is commonly suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While in the US, spent fuel is presently in its entirety, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Waste_Policy_Act" title="Nuclear Waste Policy Act">federally classified as a nuclear waste</a> and is treated similarly,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> in other countries it is largely reprocessed to produce a partially recycled fuel, known as mixed oxide fuel or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOX" title="MOX">MOX</a>. For spent fuel that does not undergo reprocessing, the most concerning isotopes are the medium-lived <a href="/wiki/Transuranium_element" title="Transuranium element">transuranic elements</a>, which are led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactor_grade_plutonium" title="Reactor grade plutonium">reactor grade plutonium</a> (half-life 24,000 years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some proposed reactor designs, such as the American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integral_Fast_Reactor" title="Integral Fast Reactor">Integral Fast Reactor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_reactor" title="Molten salt reactor">Molten salt reactor</a> can more completely use or <a href="/wiki/Burnup" title="Burnup">burnup</a> the spent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactor_grade_plutonium" title="Reactor grade plutonium">reactor grade plutonium</a> fuel and other minor actinides, generated from light water reactors, as under the designed <a href="/wiki/Fast_fission" title="Fast fission">fast fission</a> spectrum, these elements are more likely to fission and produce the aforementioned fission products in their place. This offers a potentially more attractive alternative to deep geological disposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle" title="Thorium fuel cycle">thorium fuel cycle</a> results in similar fission products, though builds up much less <a href="/wiki/Transuranium_element" title="Transuranium element">transuranic elements</a> from <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> events within a reactor and therefore spent thorium fuel, breeding the true fuel of fissile <a href="/wiki/Uranium-233" title="Uranium-233">U-233</a>, is somewhat less concerning from a radiotoxic and security standpoint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-level_radioactive_waste">Low-level radioactive waste</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Low-level_waste" title="Low-level waste">Low-level waste</a></div>\n<p>The nuclear industry also produces a large volume of low-level radioactive waste in the form of contaminated items like clothing, hand tools, water purifier resins, and (upon decommissioning) the materials of which the reactor itself is built. Low-level waste can be stored on-site until radiation levels are low enough to be disposed as ordinary waste, or it can be sent to a low-level waste disposal site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_relative_to_other_types">Waste relative to other types</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste#Naturally_occurring_radioactive_material" title="Radioactive waste">Radioactive waste &#167;&#160;Naturally occurring radioactive material</a></div>\n<p>In countries with nuclear power, radioactive wastes account for less than 1% of total industrial toxic wastes, much of which remains hazardous for long periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-wmitnfc_172-3"><a href="#cite_note-wna-wmitnfc-172">[172]</a></sup> Overall, nuclear power produces far less waste material by volume than fossil-fuel based power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> Coal-burning plants are particularly noted for producing large amounts of toxic and mildly radioactive ash due to concentrating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NORM" title="NORM">naturally occurring metals and mildly radioactive material</a> in coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> A 2008 report from <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a> concluded that coal power actually results in more radioactivity being released into the environment than nuclear power operation, and that the population <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effective_dose_(radiation_safety)" title="Effective dose (radiation safety)">effective dose</a> equivalent, or dose to the public from radiation from coal plants is 100 times as much as from the operation of nuclear plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-colmain_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-colmain-216">[216]</a></sup>\nAlthough coal ash is much less radioactive than spent nuclear fuel on a weight per weight basis, coal ash is produced in much higher quantities per unit of energy generated, and this is released directly into the environment as <a href="/wiki/Fly_ash" title="Fly ash">fly ash</a>, whereas nuclear plants use shielding to protect the environment from radioactive materials, for example, in <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry cask storage</a> vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cejournal_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-cejournal-217">[217]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_disposal">Waste disposal</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WIPP-04.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="192" data-file-width="288" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/WIPP-04.jpeg/220px-WIPP-04.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e9/WIPP-04.jpeg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WIPP-04.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The placement of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_flask#United_States" title="Nuclear flask">Nuclear waste flasks</a>, generated during US cold war activities, underground at the WIPP facility. The facility is seen as a potential demonstration, for later civilian generated spent fuel, or constituents of it.</div></div></div>\n<p>Disposal of nuclear waste is often considered the most politically divisive aspect in the lifecycle of a nuclear power facility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mont2011_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-mont2011-218">[218]</a></sup>\nPresently, waste is mainly stored at individual reactor sites and there are over 430 locations around the world where radioactive material continues to accumulate.\nSome experts suggest that centralized underground repositories which are well-managed, guarded, and monitored, would be a vast improvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mont2011_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-mont2011-218">[218]</a></sup>\nThere is an "international consensus on the advisability of storing nuclear waste in <a href="/wiki/Deep_geological_repository" title="Deep geological repository">deep geological repositories</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-go_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-go-219">[219]</a></sup> with the lack of movement of nuclear waste in the 2 billion year old <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">natural nuclear fission reactors</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oklo" title="Oklo">Oklo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a> being cited as "a source of essential information today."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg/220px-Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg/330px-Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg/440px-Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alpha-Gamma_Hot_Cell_Facility_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Most waste packaging, small-scale experimental fuel recycling chemistry and <a href="/wiki/Radiopharmaceutical" title="Radiopharmaceutical">radiopharmaceutical</a> refinement is conducted within remote-handled <a href="/wiki/Hot_cell" title="Hot cell">Hot cells</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>There are no commercial scale purpose built underground high-level waste repositories in operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-go_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-go-219">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greeninc_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-greeninc-149">[149]</a></sup> However, in Finland the <a href="/wiki/Onkalo_spent_nuclear_fuel_repository" title="Onkalo spent nuclear fuel repository">Onkalo spent nuclear fuel repository</a> is under construction as of 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Waste_Isolation_Pilot_Plant" title="Waste Isolation Pilot Plant">Waste Isolation Pilot Plant</a> (WIPP) in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> has been taking nuclear waste since 1999 from production reactors, but as the name suggests is a research and development facility.\nIn 2014 a radiation leak caused by violations in the use of chemically reactive packaging<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> brought renewed attention to the need for quality control management, along with some initial calls for more R&amp;D into the alternative methods of disposal for radioactive waste and spent fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeff_Tollefson_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeff_Tollefson-226">[226]</a></sup>\nIn 2017, the facility was formally reopened after three years of investigation and cleanup, with the resumption of new storage taking place later that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reprocessing">Reprocessing</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Plutonium_Management_and_Disposition_Agreement" title="Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement">Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plutonium_and_uranium_extraction_from_nuclear_fuel-eng.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1764" data-file-width="1565" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Plutonium_and_uranium_extraction_from_nuclear_fuel-eng.svg/240px-Plutonium_and_uranium_extraction_from_nuclear_fuel-eng.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Plutonium_and_uranium_extraction_from_nuclear_fuel-eng.svg/360px-Plutonium_and_uranium_extraction_from_nuclear_fuel-eng.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Plutonium_and_uranium_extraction_from_nuclear_fuel-eng.svg/480px-Plutonium_and_uranium_extraction_from_nuclear_fuel-eng.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plutonium_and_uranium_extraction_from_nuclear_fuel-eng.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reprocessing of <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a> by the <a href="/wiki/PUREX" title="PUREX">PUREX</a> method, first developed in the 1940s to produce plutonium for nuclear weapons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> was demonstrated commercially in Belgium to re-fuel a LWR in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuroNuclear_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-EuroNuclear-229">[229]</a></sup> This aqueous chemical process continues to be used commercially to separate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactor_grade_plutonium" title="Reactor grade plutonium">reactor grade plutonium</a> (RGPu) for reuse as MOX fuel. It remains controversial, as the separated plutonium can be used to make nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ifr_concept.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="703" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Ifr_concept.jpg/290px-Ifr_concept.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Ifr_concept.jpg/435px-Ifr_concept.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Ifr_concept.jpg/580px-Ifr_concept.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ifr_concept.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The most developed, though commercially unfielded, alternative reprocessing method, is <a href="/wiki/Pyroprocessing" title="Pyroprocessing">Pyroprocessing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> most prominently suggested as part of the metallic-fueled, <a href="/wiki/Integral_fast_reactor" title="Integral fast reactor">Integral fast reactor</a> (IFR) concept proposed in the 1990s.  After the spent fuel is dissolved in molten salt, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actinides" title="Actinides">actinides</a>, consisting mostly of plutonium and uranium, are extracted using electrorefining and/or electrowinning.  The resulting mixture of <a href="/wiki/MOX_fuel#Americium_content" title="MOX fuel">gamma and alpha emitting actinides</a> is mildly self-protecting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_reactor" title="Thermal reactor">thermal reactors</a> run on a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle#Once-through_nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">once-through fuel cycle</a>, mainly due to the low price of fresh uranium, though many can also fuel made by recycling the fissionable materials in spent nuclear fuel.  The most common fissionable material that is recycled is the reactor-grade plutonium (<i>RGPu</i>) that is extracted from spent fuel, mixed with uranium oxide and fabricated into mixed-oxide or <a href="/wiki/MOX_fuel" title="MOX fuel">MOX fuel</a>. The potential for recycling the spent fuel a second time is limited by undesirable <a href="/wiki/Neutron_economy" title="Neutron economy">neutron economy</a> issues using second-generation MOX fuel in <i>thermal</i>-reactors.  These issues do not affect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_reactor" title="Fast reactor">fast reactors</a>, which are therefore preferred in order to achieve the full energy potential of the original uranium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berrytoll_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-berrytoll-234">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEE_Spectrum_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE_Spectrum-235">[235]</a></sup> The only commercial demonstration of triple burnup to date occurred in the <a href="/wiki/Ph%C3%A9nix" title="Ph&#233;nix">Ph&#233;nix</a> <i>fast</i> reactor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because thermal LWRs remain the most common and economically competitive reactor worldwide, the most common form of commercial spent fuel recycling is to recycle the plutonium a single time as MOX fuel, as is done in France, where it is thought to increase the sustainability of the nuclear fuel cycle, reduce the attractiveness of spent fuel to theft and lower the volume of high level nuclear waste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> Reprocessing of civilian fuel from power reactors is also currently done in the United Kingdom, Russia, Japan, and India.\n</p><p>The main constituent of spent fuel from the most common light water reactor, is uranium that is slightly more enriched than natural uranium, which can be recycled, though there is a lower incentive to do so. Most of this "recovered uranium",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> or at times referred to as <a href="/wiki/Reprocessed_uranium" title="Reprocessed uranium">reprocessed uranium</a>, remains in storage. It can however be used in a fast reactor, used directly as fuel in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CANDU" title="CANDU">CANDU</a> reactors, or re-enriched for another cycle through an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LWR" title="LWR">LWR</a>. The direct use of recovered uranium to fuel a CANDU reactor was first demonstrated at <a href="/wiki/Qinshan_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Qinshan Nuclear Power Plant">Quishan, China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> The first re-enriched uranium reload to fuel a commercial LWR, occurred in 1994 at the <a href="/wiki/Cruas_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Cruas Nuclear Power Plant">Cruas unit 4, France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> Re-enriching of reprocessed uranium is common in France and Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA3_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNA3-242">[242]</a></sup> When reprocessed uranium, namely <a href="/wiki/Uranium-236" title="Uranium-236">Uranium-236</a>, is part of the fuel of LWRs, it generates a spent fuel and plutonium isotope stream with greater inherent self-protection, than the once-thru fuel cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While reprocessing offers the potential recovery of up to 95% of the remaining uranium and plutonium fuel, in spent nuclear fuel and a reduction in long term radioactivity within the remaining waste. Reprocessing has been politically controversial because of the potential to contribute to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a> and varied perceptions of increasing the vulnerability to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">nuclear terrorism</a> and because of its higher fuel cost, compared to the once-through fuel cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berrytoll_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-berrytoll-234">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bas2011_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-bas2011-246">[246]</a></sup> Similarly, while reprocessing reduces the volume of high-level waste, it does not reduce the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission products</a> that are the primary residual heat generating and radioactive substances for the first few centuries outside the reactor, thus still requiring an almost identical container-spacing for the initial first few hundred years, within proposed geological waste isolation facilities.\n</p><p>In the United States, spent nuclear fuel is currently not reprocessed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA3_242-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNA3-242">[242]</a></sup> A major recommendation of the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America%27s_Nuclear_Future" title="Blue Ribbon Commission on America\'s Nuclear Future">Blue Ribbon Commission on America\'s Nuclear Future</a> was that "the United States should undertake...one or more permanent deep geological facilities for the safe disposal of spent fuel and high-level nuclear waste".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America&#8217;s_Nuclear_Future_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America&#8217;s_Nuclear_Future-247">[247]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The French <a href="/wiki/La_Hague_site" title="La Hague site">La Hague reprocessing facility</a> has operated commercially since 1976 and is responsible for half the world\'s reprocessing as of 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> Having produced MOX fuel from spent fuel derived from France, Japan, Germany, Belgium, Switzerland, Italy, Spain and the Netherlands, with the non-recyclable part of the spent fuel eventually sent back to the user nation.
