More than 32,000 tonnes of spent fuel had been reprocessed as of 2015, with the majority from France, 17% from Germany, and 9% from Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> Once a source of criticism from Greenpeace, more recently the organization have ceased attempting to criticize the facility on technical grounds, having succeeded at performing the process without serious incidents that have been frequent at other such facilities around the world. In the past, the antinuclear movement argued that reprocessing would not be technically or economically feasible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fairley_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-fairley-250">[250]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_decommissioning">Nuclear decommissioning</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">nuclear decommissioning</a></div>\n<p>The financial costs of every nuclear power plant continues for some time after the facility has finished generating its last useful electricity. Once no longer economically viable, nuclear reactors and uranium enrichment facilities are generally decommissioned, returning the facility and its parts to a safe enough level to be entrusted for other uses, such as <a href="/wiki/Greenfield_status" title="Greenfield status">greenfield status</a>.\nAfter a cooling-off period that may last decades, reactor core materials are dismantled and cut into small pieces to be packed in containers for interim storage or <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a> experiments.\n</p><p>In the United States a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Waste_Policy_Act" title="Nuclear Waste Policy Act">Nuclear Waste Policy Act</a> and Nuclear Decommissioning Trust Fund is legally required, with utilities banking 0.1 to 0.2 cents/kWh during operations to fund future decommissioning. They must report regularly to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> (NRC) on the status of their decommissioning funds. About 70% of the total estimated cost of decommissioning all U.S. nuclear power reactors has already been collected (on the basis of the average cost of $320 million per reactor-steam turbine unit).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the United States in 2011, there are 13 reactors that had permanently shut down and are in some phase of decommissioning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sov11_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-sov11-252">[252]</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Yankee_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Connecticut Yankee Nuclear Power Plant">Connecticut Yankee Nuclear Power Plant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Rowe_Nuclear_Power_Station" title="Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station">Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station</a> having completed the process in 2006&#8211;2007, after ceasing commercial electricity production circa 1992.\nThe majority of the 15 years, was used to allow the station to naturally cool-down on its own, which makes the manual disassembly process both safer and cheaper.\nDecommissioning at nuclear sites which have experienced a serious accident are the most expensive and time-consuming.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Installed_capacity_and_electricity_production">Installed capacity and electricity production</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">Nuclear power by country</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">List of nuclear reactors</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_percentage.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nuclear_power_percentage.svg/220px-Nuclear_power_percentage.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nuclear_power_percentage.svg/330px-Nuclear_power_percentage.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nuclear_power_percentage.svg/440px-Nuclear_power_percentage.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_percentage.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Share of electricity produced by nuclear power in the world</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_station.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Nuclear_power_station.svg/220px-Nuclear_power_station.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Nuclear_power_station.svg/330px-Nuclear_power_station.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Nuclear_power_station.svg/440px-Nuclear_power_station.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_station.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The status of nuclear power globally (click image for legend)</div></div></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="967" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg/220px-Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg/330px-Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg/440px-Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Net <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generation" title="Electrical generation">electrical generation</a> by source and growth from 1980 to 2010. (Brown) &#8211; fossil fuels. (Red) &#8211; Fission. (Green) &#8211; "all renewables". In terms of energy generated between 1980 and 2010, the contribution from fission grew the fastest.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_history.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nuclear_power_history.svg/220px-Nuclear_power_history.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nuclear_power_history.svg/330px-Nuclear_power_history.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nuclear_power_history.svg/440px-Nuclear_power_history.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The rate of new construction builds for civilian fission-electric reactors essentially halted in the late 1980s, with the effects of accidents having a <a href="/wiki/Chilling_effect" title="Chilling effect">chilling effect</a>. Increased <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a> realizations in existing reactors was primarily responsible for the continuing increase in electrical energy produced during this period. The halting of new builds c. 1985, resulted in greater fossil fuel generation, see above graph.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="2050" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png/220px-Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png/330px-Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png/440px-Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Electricity generation trends in the top five fission-energy producing countries (US EIA data)</div></div></div></div>\n<p>Nuclear fission power stations, excluding the contribution from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">naval nuclear fission reactors</a>, provided 11% of the world\'s electricity in 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd_pdf_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-oecd_pdf-253">[253]</a></sup> somewhat less than that generated by <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydro-electric stations</a> at 16%.\nSince electricity accounts for about 25% of humanity\'s energy usage with the majority of the rest coming from fossil fuel reliant sectors such as transport, manufacture and home heating, nuclear fission\'s contribution to the <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">global final energy consumption</a> was about 2.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup>\nThis is a little more than the combined global electricity production from wind, solar, <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> and geothermal power, which together provided 2% of global final energy consumption in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition, there were approximately 140 naval vessels using <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_propulsion" title="Nuclear propulsion">nuclear propulsion</a> in operation, powered by about 180 reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nuclear power\'s share of global electricity production has fallen from 16.5% in 1997 to about 10% in 2017, in large part because the economics of nuclear power have become more difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft-20180903_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft-20180903-258">[258]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Regional differences in the use of nuclear power are large.\nThe United States produces the most nuclear energy in the world, with nuclear power providing 20% of the electricity it consumes, while France produces the highest percentage of its electrical energy from nuclear reactors&#8212;72% as of 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>\nIn the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> as a whole nuclear power provides 30% of the electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup>\nNuclear power is the single largest low-carbon electricity source in the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-issues_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-issues-260">[260]</a></sup> and accounts for two-thirds of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>\'s low-carbon electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">Nuclear energy policy</a> differs among European Union countries, and some, such as Austria, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, Ireland and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Italy" title="Nuclear power in Italy">Italy</a>, have no active nuclear power stations.\n</p><p>Many military and some civilian (such as some <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreakers</a>) ships use <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">nuclear marine propulsion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup>\nA few space vehicles have been launched using <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>: 33 reactors belong to the Soviet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RORSAT" title="RORSAT">RORSAT</a> series and one was the American <a href="/wiki/SNAP-10A" title="SNAP-10A">SNAP-10A</a>.\n</p><p>International research is continuing into additional uses of process heat such as <a href="/wiki/Water_splitting#Nuclear-thermal" title="Water splitting">hydrogen production</a> (in support of a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">hydrogen economy</a>), for <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">desalinating</a> sea water, and for use in <a href="/wiki/District_heating" title="District heating">district heating</a> systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_space">Use in space</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MMRTG_hot_cell_fueling_(6348365583).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/MMRTG_hot_cell_fueling_%286348365583%29.jpg/220px-MMRTG_hot_cell_fueling_%286348365583%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/MMRTG_hot_cell_fueling_%286348365583%29.jpg/330px-MMRTG_hot_cell_fueling_%286348365583%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/MMRTG_hot_cell_fueling_%286348365583%29.jpg/440px-MMRTG_hot_cell_fueling_%286348365583%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MMRTG_hot_cell_fueling_(6348365583).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The loading of the Plutonium-238 based MMRTG into the <i>Mars Curiosity</i> rover. Assembled in a <a href="/wiki/Hot_cell" title="Hot cell">Hot cell</a> at <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory" title="Idaho National Laboratory">Idaho National Laboratory</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Msl-MMRTG.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="327" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Msl-MMRTG.jpg/220px-Msl-MMRTG.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/ba/Msl-MMRTG.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Msl-MMRTG.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Multi-mission_radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Multi-mission radioisotope thermoelectric generator">Multi-mission radioisotope thermoelectric generator</a> (MMRTG), used in several space missions such as the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)">Curiosity Mars rover</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_space" title="Nuclear power in space">Nuclear power in space</a></div>\n<p>Both <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fission</a> and fusion appear promising for <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">space propulsion</a> applications, generating higher mission velocities with less <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction_mass" title="Reaction mass">reaction mass</a>. This is due to the much higher energy density of nuclear reactions: some 7 orders of magnitude (10,000,000 times) more energetic than the chemical reactions which power the current generation of rockets.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">Radioactive decay</a> has been used on a relatively small scale (few kW), mostly to power <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_mission" title="Space mission">space missions</a> and experiments by using <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> such as those developed at <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory#Space_and_Security_Power_Systems_Facility" title="Idaho National Laboratory">Idaho National Laboratory</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_nuclear_power_plants" title="Economics of nuclear power plants">Economics of nuclear power plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_companies_in_the_nuclear_sector" title="List of companies in the nuclear sector">List of companies in the nuclear sector</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_electricity_by_source" title="Cost of electricity by source">cost of electricity by source</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1440" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/220px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/330px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/440px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Ikata_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Ikata Nuclear Power Plant">Ikata Nuclear Power Plant</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">pressurized water reactor</a> that cools by utilizing a secondary coolant <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heat exchanger</a> with a large body of water, an alternative cooling approach to large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooling_towers" title="Cooling towers">cooling towers</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The economics of new nuclear power plants is a controversial subject, since there are diverging views on this topic, and multibillion-dollar investments depend on the choice of an energy source.\nNuclear power plants typically have high capital costs for building the plant, but low fuel costs.\nComparison with other power generation methods is strongly dependent on assumptions about construction timescales and capital financing for nuclear plants as well as the future costs of fossil fuels and renewables as well as for energy storage solutions for intermittent power sources.\nOn the other hand, measures to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" title="Mitigation of global warming">mitigate</a> <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, such as a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions_trading" title="Carbon emissions trading">carbon emissions trading</a>, may favor the economics of nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Analysis of the economics of nuclear power must also take into account who bears the risks of future uncertainties.\nTo date all operating nuclear power plants have been developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalized" title="Nationalized">state-owned</a> or <a href="/wiki/Regulated_market" title="Regulated market">regulated</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_utility" title="Electric utility">electric utility</a> monopolies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft-20100912_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft-20100912-266">[266]</a></sup>\nMany countries have now liberalized the <a href="/wiki/Electricity_market" title="Electricity market">electricity market</a> where these risks, and the risk of cheaper competitors emerging before capital costs are recovered, are borne by plant suppliers and operators rather than consumers, which leads to a significantly different evaluation of the economics of new nuclear power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT-2003_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-2003-267">[267]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nuclear power plants, though capable of some grid-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Load_following" title="Load following">load following</a>, are typically run as much as possible to keep the cost of the generated electrical energy as low as possible, supplying mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base-load" title="Base-load">base-load</a> electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Internationally the price of nuclear plants rose 15% annually in 1970&#8211;1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\nWith <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">PWR</a> stations, having total costs in 2012 of about $96 per megawatt hour (MWh), most of which involves capital construction costs, compared with (in 2018) solar power at $36-44 per MWh, (in 2018) onshore wind at $29-56 per MWH and natural gas at the low end at $64 per MWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup>\nThe Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster, is expected to increase the costs of operating and new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LWR" title="LWR">LWR</a> power stations, due to increased requirements for on-site spent fuel management and elevated design basis threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Accidents.2C_attacks_and_safety"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents,_attacks_and_safety">Accidents, attacks and safety</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety_and_security" title="Nuclear safety and security">Nuclear safety and security</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor_safety_system" title="Nuclear reactor safety system">Nuclear reactor safety system</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="583" data-file-width="784" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG/220px-Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG/330px-Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG/440px-Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Decay_heat_illustration2.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reactor <a href="/wiki/Decay_heat" title="Decay heat">decay heat</a> as fraction of full power after the reactor shutdown, using two different correlations. Reactors need cooling after the shutdown of the fission reactions, to prevent a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_melt_accident" title="Core melt accident">core melt accident</a>. The loss of cooling caused the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_accident" title="Fukushima accident">Fukushima accident</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Nuclear reactors have three unique characteristics that affect their safety, as compared to other power plants.\nFirstly, intensely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive_material" title="Radioactive material">radioactive materials</a> are present in a nuclear reactor. Their release to the environment could be hazardous.\nSecondly, the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission_product" title="Nuclear fission product">fission products</a>, which make up most of the intensely radioactive substances in the reactor, continue to generate a significant amount of <a href="/wiki/Decay_heat" title="Decay heat">decay heat</a> even after the fission <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">chain reaction</a> has stopped. If the heat cannot be removed from the reactor, the fuel rods may overheat and release radioactive materials.\nThirdly, a <a href="/wiki/Criticality_accident" title="Criticality accident">rapid increase of the reactor power</a> is possible if the chain reaction cannot be controlled in certain reactor designs.\nThese three characteristics have to be taken into account when designing nuclear reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEAsafety_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEAsafety-273">[273]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Reactors are designed so that an uncontrolled increase of the reactor power is prevented by natural feedback mechanisms: if the temperature or the amount of steam in the reactor increases, the fission power inherently decreases. The chain reaction can be manually stopped by inserting <a href="/wiki/Control_rod" title="Control rod">control rods</a> into the reactor core. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_core_cooling_system" title="Emergency core cooling system">Emergency core cooling systems</a> can remove the decay heat from the reactor if normal cooling systems fail<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup>. Multiple physical barriers limit the release of radioactive materials to the environment even in the case of an accident. The last barrier is the <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEAsafety_273-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAEAsafety-273">[273]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents">Accidents</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="1095" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/220px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/330px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/440px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Following the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a>, the world\'s worst <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_accident" title="Nuclear accident">nuclear accident</a> since 1986, 50,000 households were displaced after <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> leaked into the air, soil and sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup> Radiation checks led to bans of some shipments of vegetables and fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">Energy accidents</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents">Nuclear and radiation accidents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_nuclear_disasters_and_radioactive_incidents" title="Lists of nuclear disasters and radioactive incidents">Lists of nuclear disasters and radioactive incidents</a></div>\n<p>Some serious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_by_death_toll" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents by death toll">nuclear and radiation accidents</a> have occurred.\nThe severity of nuclear accidents is generally classified using the <a href="/wiki/International_Nuclear_Event_Scale" title="International Nuclear Event Scale">International Nuclear Event Scale</a> (INES) introduced by the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a> (IAEA).\nThe scale ranks anomalous events or accidents on a scale from 0 (a deviation from normal operation that poses no safety risk) to 7 (a major accident with widespread effects).\nThere have been 3 accidents of level 5 or higher in the civilian nuclear power industry, two of which, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chernobyl_accident" title="Chernobyl accident">Chernobyl accident</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_accident" title="Fukushima accident">Fukushima accident</a>, are ranked at level 7.\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chernobyl_accident" title="Chernobyl accident">Chernobyl accident</a> in 1986 caused approximately 50 deaths from direct and indirect effects, and some <a href="/wiki/Acute_radiation_syndrome" title="Acute radiation syndrome">temporary serious injuries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2012_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2012-277">[277]</a></sup>\nThe future predicted mortality from cancer increases, is usually estimated at some 4000 in the decades to come.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_2008_D_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_2008_D-279">[279]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup> A higher number of the routinely treatable <a href="/wiki/Thyroid_cancer" title="Thyroid cancer">Thyroid cancer</a>, set to be the only type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Causal" title="Causal">causal</a> cancer, will likely be seen in future large studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_accident" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident</a> was caused by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2011_Tohoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tohoku earthquake and tsunami">2011 Tohoku earthquake and tsunami</a>.\nThe accident has not caused any radiation related deaths, but resulted in radioactive contamination of surrounding areas.\nThe difficult <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_disaster_cleanup" title="Fukushima disaster cleanup">Fukushima disaster cleanup</a> will take 40 or more years, and is expected to cost tens of billions of dollars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richard_Schiffman_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richard_Schiffman-282">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_Fackler_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Fackler-283">[283]</a></sup>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> in 1979 was a smaller scale accident, rated at INES level 5.\nThere were no direct or indirect deaths caused by the accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timenuke_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-284">[284]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to Benjamin K. Sovacool, fission <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accidents</a> ranked first among energy sources in terms of their total economic cost, accounting for 41 percent of all property damage attributed to energy accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup>\nAnother analysis presented in the international journal <i>Human and Ecological Risk Assessment</i> found that coal, oil, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_petroleum_gas" title="Liquid petroleum gas">Liquid petroleum gas</a> and hydroelectric accidents (primarily due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banqiao_dam" title="Banqiao dam">Banqiao dam</a> burst) have resulted in greater economic impacts than nuclear power accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tandfonline1_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-tandfonline1-286">[286]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nuclear power works under an <a href="/wiki/Insurance" title="Insurance">insurance</a> framework that limits or structures accident liabilities in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Convention_on_Third_Party_Liability_in_the_Field_of_Nuclear_Energy" title="Paris Convention on Third Party Liability in the Field of Nuclear Energy">Paris convention on nuclear third-party liability</a>, the Brussels supplementary convention, the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_Civil_Liability_for_Nuclear_Damage" title="Vienna Convention on Civil Liability for Nuclear Damage">Vienna convention on civil liability for nuclear damage</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Price%E2%80%93Anderson_Nuclear_Industries_Indemnity_Act" title="Price&#8211;Anderson Nuclear Industries Indemnity Act">Price-Anderson Act</a> in the United States.\nIt is often argued that this potential shortfall in liability represents an external cost not included in the cost of nuclear electricity; but the cost is small, amounting to about 0.1% of the levelized cost of electricity, according to a CBO study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup>\nThese beyond-regular-insurance costs for worst-case scenarios are not unique to nuclear power, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power" title="Hydroelectric power">hydroelectric power</a> plants are similarly not fully insured against a catastrophic event such as the <a href="/wiki/Banqiao_Dam" title="Banqiao Dam">Banqiao Dam</a> disaster, where 11 million people lost their homes and from 30,000 to 200,000 people died, or large <a href="/wiki/Dam_failure" title="Dam failure">dam failures</a> in general. As private insurers base dam insurance premiums on limited scenarios, major disaster insurance in this sector is likewise provided by the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span></h3>\n<p>In terms of lives lost per unit of energy generated, nuclear power has caused fewer accidental deaths per unit of energy generated than all other major sources of energy generation.\nEnergy produced by coal, petroleum, natural gas and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a> has caused more deaths per unit of energy generated due to air pollution and <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accidents</a>.\nThis is found when comparing the immediate deaths from other energy sources to both the immediate nuclear related deaths from accidents<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-without_the_hot_air_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-without_the_hot_air-290">[290]</a></sup> and also including the latent, or predicted, indirect cancer deaths from nuclear <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accidents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theage2006_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-theage2006-291">[291]</a></sup>\nWhen the combined immediate and indirect fatalities from nuclear power and all fossil fuels are compared, including fatalities resulting from the mining of the necessary natural resources to power generation and to air pollution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarkandyaWilkinson2007_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-MarkandyaWilkinson2007-9">[9]</a></sup> the use of nuclear power has been calculated to have prevented about 1.8 million deaths between 1971 and 2009, by reducing the proportion of energy that would otherwise have been generated by fossil fuels, and is projected to continue to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-292">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[293]</a></sup>\nFollowing the 2011 Fukushima nuclear disaster, it has been estimated that if Japan had never adopted nuclear power, accidents and pollution from coal or gas plants would have caused more lost years of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Forced evacuation from a nuclear accident may lead to social isolation, anxiety, depression, psychosomatic medical problems, reckless behavior, even suicide.\nSuch was the outcome of the 1986 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chernobyl_nuclear_disaster" title="Chernobyl nuclear disaster">Chernobyl nuclear disaster</a> in Ukraine.\nA comprehensive 2005 study concluded that "the mental health impact of Chernobyl is the largest public health problem unleashed by the accident to date".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riv12_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-riv12-295">[295]</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/Frank_N._von_Hippel" title="Frank N. von Hippel">Frank N. von Hippel</a>, an American scientist, commented on the 2011 Fukushima nuclear disaster, saying that a disproportionate <a href="/wiki/Radiophobia" title="Radiophobia">radiophobia</a>, or "fear of ionizing radiation could have long-term psychological effects on a large portion of the population in the contaminated areas".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frank_N._von_Hippel_27&#8211;36_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frank_N._von_Hippel_27&#8211;36-296">[296]</a></sup>\nA 2015 report in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lancet" title="The Lancet">Lancet</a></i> explained that serious impacts of nuclear accidents were often not directly attributable to radiation exposure, but rather social and psychological effects.\nEvacuation and long-term displacement of affected populations created problems for many people, especially the elderly and hospital patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup>\nIn January 2015, the number of Fukushima evacuees was around 119,000, compared with a peak of around 164,000 in June 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attacks_and_sabotage">Attacks and sabotage</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_of_nuclear_plants_to_attack" title="Vulnerability of nuclear plants to attack">Vulnerability of nuclear plants to attack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">Nuclear terrorism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety_in_the_United_States" title="Nuclear safety in the United States">Nuclear safety in the United States</a></div>\n<p>Terrorists could target <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plants</a> in an attempt to release <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">radioactive contamination</a> into the community. The United States 9/11 Commission has said that nuclear power plants were potential targets originally considered for the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11, 2001 attacks</a>. An attack on a reactor\'s <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a> could also be serious, as these pools are less protected than the reactor core. The release of radioactivity could lead to thousands of near-term deaths and greater numbers of long-term fatalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fas12_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-fas12-299">[299]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the United States, the NRC carries out "Force on Force" (FOF) exercises at all nuclear power plant sites at least once every three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fas12_299-1"><a href="#cite_note-fas12-299">[299]</a></sup>\nIn the United States, plants are surrounded by a double row of tall fences which are electronically monitored.\nThe plant grounds are patrolled by a sizeable force of armed guards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Insider sabotage is also a threat because insiders can observe and work around security measures.\nSuccessful insider crimes depended on the perpetrators\' observation and knowledge of security vulnerabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[301]</a></sup>\nA fire caused 5&#8211;10 million dollars worth of damage to New York\'s <a href="/wiki/Indian_Point_Energy_Center" title="Indian Point Energy Center">Indian Point Energy Center</a> in 1971.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\nThe arsonist turned out to be a plant maintenance worker.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some reactors overseas have also reported varying levels of sabotage by workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amory_Lovins_2001_145&#8211;146_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amory_Lovins_2001_145&#8211;146-302">[302]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_proliferation">Nuclear proliferation</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">Nuclear proliferation</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Plutonium_Management_and_Disposition_Agreement" title="Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement">Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="555" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/220px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/330px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/440px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>United States and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a>/Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a> stockpiles, 1945&#8211;2006. The <a href="/wiki/Megatons_to_Megawatts_Program" title="Megatons to Megawatts Program">Megatons to Megawatts Program</a> was the main driving force behind the sharp reduction in the quantity of nuclear weapons worldwide since the cold war ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-303">[303]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usec.com_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-usec.com-304">[304]</a></sup> However, without an increase in nuclear reactors and greater demand for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fissile" title="Fissile">fissile</a> fuel, the cost of dismantling has dissuaded Russia from continuing their disarmament.</div></div></div>\n<p>Many technologies and materials associated with the creation of a nuclear power program have a dual-use capability, in that they can be used to make <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a> if a country chooses to do so. When this happens a nuclear power program can become a route leading to a nuclear weapon or a public annex to a "secret" weapons program. The concern over <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_program_of_Iran" title="Nuclear program of Iran">Iran\'s nuclear activities</a> is a case in point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dfall2009_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-dfall2009-305">[305]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of April 2012 there were <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">thirty one countries</a> that have civil nuclear power plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup> of which <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_nuclear_weapons" title="List of states with nuclear weapons">nine have nuclear weapons</a>, with the vast majority of these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_state" title="Nuclear weapons state">nuclear weapons states</a> having first produced weapons, before commercial fission electricity stations.\nMoreover, the re-purposing of civilian nuclear industries for military purposes would be a breach of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-proliferation_treaty" title="Non-proliferation treaty">Non-proliferation treaty</a>, of which 190 countries adhere to.\n</p><p>A fundamental goal for global security is to minimize the nuclear proliferation risks associated with the expansion of nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dfall2009_305-1"><a href="#cite_note-dfall2009-305">[305]</a></sup>\nThe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_Nuclear_Energy_Partnership" title="Global Nuclear Energy Partnership">Global Nuclear Energy Partnership</a> was an international effort to create a distribution network in which developing countries in need of energy would receive <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> at a discounted rate, in exchange for that nation agreeing to forgo their own indigenous develop of a uranium enrichment program.\nThe France-based <a href="/wiki/Eurodif" title="Eurodif">Eurodif</a>/<i>European Gaseous Diffusion Uranium Enrichment Consortium</i> is a program that successfully implemented this concept, with <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Spain" title="Nuclear power in Spain">Spain</a> and other countries without enrichment facilities buying a share of the fuel produced at the French controlled enrichment facility, but without a transfer of technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup>\nIran was an early participant from 1974, and remains a shareholder of Eurodif via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sofidif" title="Sofidif">Sofidif</a>.\n</p><p>A 2009 United Nations report said that:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>the revival of interest in nuclear power could result in the worldwide dissemination of uranium enrichment and spent fuel reprocessing technologies, which present obvious risks of proliferation as these technologies can produce fissile materials that are directly usable in nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bks2011_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-bks2011-308">[308]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>On the other hand, power reactors can also reduce nuclear weapons arsenals when military grade nuclear materials are reprocessed to be used as fuel in nuclear power plants.\nThe <a href="/wiki/Megatons_to_Megawatts_Program" title="Megatons to Megawatts Program">Megatons to Megawatts Program</a>, the brainchild of Thomas Neff of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014-309">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup> is the single most successful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-proliferation" title="Non-proliferation">non-proliferation</a> program to date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_303-1"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-303">[303]</a></sup>\nUp to 2005, the Megatons to Megawatts Program had processed $8 billion of high enriched, weapons grade uranium into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_enriched_uranium" title="Low enriched uranium">low enriched uranium</a> suitable as nuclear fuel for commercial fission reactors by diluting it with <a href="/wiki/Natural_uranium" title="Natural uranium">natural uranium</a>.\nThis corresponds to the elimination of 10,000 nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[311]</a></sup>\nFor approximately two decades, this material generated nearly 10 percent of all the electricity consumed in the United States (about half of all U.S. nuclear electricity generated) with a total of around 7 trillion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hours" title="Kilowatt-hours">kilowatt-hours</a> of electricity produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-312">[312]</a></sup> Enough energy to energize the entire United States electric grid for about two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014_309-1"><a href="#cite_note-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014-309">[309]</a></sup> In total it is estimated to have cost $17 billion, a "bargain for US ratepayers", with Russia profiting $12 billion from the deal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_312-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-312">[312]</a></sup> Much needed profit for the <a href="/wiki/Rosatom" title="Rosatom">Russian nuclear oversight industry</a>, which after the collapse of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_economy" title="Soviet economy">Soviet economy</a>, had difficulties paying for the maintenance and security of the Russian Federations highly enriched uranium and warheads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014_309-2"><a href="#cite_note-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014-309">[309]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Megatons to Megawatts Program was hailed as a major success by anti-nuclear weapon advocates as it has largely been the driving force behind the sharp reduction in the quantity of nuclear weapons worldwide since the cold war ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_303-2"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-303">[303]</a></sup>\nHowever without an increase in nuclear reactors and greater demand for fissile fuel, the cost of dismantling and down blending has dissuaded Russia from continuing their disarmament.\nAs of 2013 Russia appears to not be interested in extending the program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[313]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact<span id="Environmental_issues"></span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_nuclear_power" title="Environmental impact of nuclear power">Environmental impact of nuclear power</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_emissions">Carbon emissions</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="773" data-file-width="1244" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png/220px-CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png/330px-CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png/440px-CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CO2_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IPCC.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions of electricity supply technologies, median values calculated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPCC" title="IPCC">IPCC</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-314">[314]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Nuclear power is one of the leading <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_carbon_power_generation" title="Low carbon power generation">low carbon power generation</a> methods of producing <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>, and in terms of <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">total life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions per unit of energy generated</a>, has emission values comparable to or lower than <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nrel.gov_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nrel.gov-315">[315]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[316]</a></sup>\nA 2014 analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> literature by the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) reported that the embodied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" title="Life cycle assessment">total life-cycle</a> <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">emission intensity</a> of fission electricity has a median value of 12&#160;g CO<sub>2</sub><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">eq</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kWh</a>, which is the lowest out of all commercial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baseload" title="Baseload">baseload</a> energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_314-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-314">[314]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report.mitigation2014.org_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-report.mitigation2014.org-317">[317]</a></sup>\nThis is contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> at 820 and 490&#160;g CO<sub>2</sub> eq/kWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_314-2"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-314">[314]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report.mitigation2014.org_317-1"><a href="#cite_note-report.mitigation2014.org-317">[317]</a></sup>\nFrom the beginning of its commercialization in the 1970s, nuclear power has prevented the emission of about 64 billion tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">carbon dioxide equivalent</a> that would have otherwise resulted from the burning of fossil fuels in <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power stations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889&#8211;4895_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889&#8211;4895-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation">Radiation</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_no_threshold_model" title="Linear no threshold model">Linear no threshold model</a></div>\n<p>The variation in a person\'s absorbed natural <a href="/wiki/Background_radiation" title="Background radiation">background radiation</a>, averages 2.4&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MSv" title="MSv">mSv</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annum" title="Annum">a</a> globally but frequently varies between 1&#160;mSv/a and 13&#160;mSv/a depending in most part on the geology a person resides upon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-318">[318]</a></sup> According to the United Nations (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNSCEAR" title="UNSCEAR">UNSCEAR</a>), regular NPP/nuclear power plant operations including the nuclear fuel cycle, increases this amount to 0.0002 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millisievert" title="Millisievert">millisieverts</a> (mSv) per year of public exposure as a global average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_318-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-318">[318]</a></sup>\nThe average dose from operating NPPs to the local populations around them is <i>less than</i> 0.0001 mSv/a.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_318-2"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-318">[318]</a></sup> The average dose to those living within 50&#160;miles of a <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> power plant is over three times this dose, 0.0003 mSv/a.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[319]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of a 2008 report, Chernobyl resulted in the most affected surrounding populations and male recovery personnel receiving an average initial 50 to 100 mSv over a few hours to weeks, while the remaining global legacy of the worst nuclear power plant accident in average exposure is 0.002&#160;mSv/a and is continually dropping at the decaying rate, from the initial high of 0.04&#160;mSv per person averaged over the entire populace of the Northern Hemisphere in the year of the accident in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_318-3"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-318">[318]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_energy_and_nuclear_power">Renewable energy and nuclear power</span></h2>\n<table class="box-POV plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-POV" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" width="45"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrality</a> of this section is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NPOV_dispute" title="Wikipedia:NPOV dispute">disputed</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Nuclear_power#Section_titled_Renewable_energy_vs_nuclear_power" title="Talk:Nuclear power">talk page</a>. Please do not remove this message until <a href="/wiki/Template:POV#When_to_remove" title="Template:POV">conditions to do so are met</a>.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_debate" title="Renewable energy debate">Renewable energy debate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_wind_power#Pollution_&amp;_effects_on_the_grid" title="Environmental impact of wind power">Environmental impact of wind power &#167;&#160;Pollution &amp; effects on the grid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity#Comparison_and_interactions_with_other_methods_of_power_generation" title="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity &#167;&#160;Comparison and interactions with other methods of power generation</a></div>\n<p>Slowing <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> requires a transition to a <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_economy" title="Low-carbon economy">low-carbon economy</a>, mainly by burning far less <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>. Limiting global warming to 1.5 degrees C is technically possible if no new fossil fuel power plants are built from 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[320]</a></sup> This has generated considerable interest and dispute in determining the best path forward to rapidly replace fossil-based fuels in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_energy_consumption" title="Global energy consumption">global energy mix</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[321]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup> with the intense academic debate, having notably involved the <a href="/wiki/Mark_Z._Jacobson#Criticism_and_lawsuit" title="Mark Z. Jacobson">filing of a lawsuit</a> by one of the lead advocates for a proposed <a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a> future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[323]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GTM-NewFront_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-GTM-NewFront-325">[325]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="PieChartTemplate thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:200px">\n<div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black">\n<div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:#00CC4B"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 791.58150883065px; border-left-color:#7C6250"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#7C6250"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#7C6250"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:100px; border-width:80.901699437495px 58.778525229247px 0 0; border-top-color:#de2821"></div>\n<div transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:0; border-width:0 32.491969623291px 100px 0; border-right-color:#313c42"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;right:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 100px 0;border-color:#313c42"></div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<p>World total <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_energy_consumption" title="Primary energy consumption">primary energy consumption</a>, energy for heating, transport, electricity, by source in 2015 was 87% fossil fueled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[326]</a></sup> In the period of 1999 to 2015, this fossil fuel percentage has remained at 87%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[327]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[328]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #313c42; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Coal (30%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ef8e39; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Natural Gas (24%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #005CE6; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Hydro (<a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewables</a>) (7%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #de2821; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Nuclear (4%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #7C6250; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Oil (33%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00CC4B; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Others (<a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewables</a>) (2%)</div>\n</div>\n</div></div>\n<p>While an approximate doubling of present world hydro capacity is considered possible in developing nations, in western nations by contrast, the economically feasible geography for new hydropower is lacking, with every geographically suitable area largely already exploited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[329]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Proponents of wind and solar energy claim these resources alone could eliminate the need for nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GTM-NewFront_325-1"><a href="#cite_note-GTM-NewFront-325">[325]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[330]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_060420-N-9621S-004_The_guided-missile_cruiser_USS_Monterey_(CG_61)_conducts_a_fueling_at_sea_(FAS)_with_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_George_Washington_(CVN_73).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="157"
