1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/US_Navy_060420-N-9621S-004_The_guided-missile_cruiser_USS_Monterey_%28CG_61%29_conducts_a_fueling_at_sea_%28FAS%29_with_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_George_Washington_%28CVN_73%29.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_060420-N-9621S-004_The_guided-missile_cruiser_USS_Monterey_(CG_61)_conducts_a_fueling_at_sea_(FAS)_with_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_George_Washington_(CVN_73).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nuclear powered aircraft carriers, presently require as depicted, jet-fuel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Replenishment_at_sea" title="Replenishment at sea">replenishment at sea</a> operations, by expensive <a href="/wiki/Replenishment_oiler" title="Replenishment oiler">replenishment oilers</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_Research_Laboratory" title="Naval Research Laboratory">Naval Research Laboratory</a> team led by <a href="/wiki/Heather_Willauer" title="Heather Willauer">Heather Willauer</a> has developed a process that is designed to use the ample electrical power onboard carriers for alternative in-situ synthesis of jet-fuel from its chemical building blocks by extracting the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) in <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a> in tandem with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> (H<sub>2</sub>) and recombining the two into long chain <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> liquids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jetsea_331-0"><a href="#cite_note-jetsea-331">[331]</a></sup> Writing in the <i>Journal of Renewable Sustainable Energy</i>, in 2012, Willauer estimated that the &#160;<a href="/wiki/Carbon-neutral_fuel#Renewable_and_nuclear_energy_costs" title="Carbon-neutral fuel">carbon neutral jet fuel</a> for Navy and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps_Aviation" title="United States Marine Corps Aviation">Marine aviation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[332]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[333]</a></sup> could be synthesized from seawater in quantities up to 100,000&#160;US&#160;gal (380,000&#160;L) per day, at a cost of three to six U.S. dollars per gallon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[334]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[335]</a></sup> The U.S. Navy is expected to deploy the technology some time in the 2020s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[336]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Some analysts argue that conventional renewable energy sources, wind and solar do not offer the scalability necessary for a large scale decarbonization of the electric grid, mainly due to <a href="/wiki/Intermittency" title="Intermittency">intermittency</a>-related considerations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kloor_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kloor-337">[337]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[338]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[339]</a></sup> Along with other commentators who have questioned the  links between the anti-nuclear movement and the fossil fuel industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[340]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[341]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[342]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[343]</a></sup>  These commentators point, in support of the assessment, to the expansion of the coal burning <a href="/wiki/Lippendorf_Power_Station" title="Lippendorf Power Station">Lippendorf Power Station</a> in Germany and in 2015 the opening of a large, 1730 MW <a href="/wiki/List_of_power_stations_in_Germany#Thermal" title="List of power stations in Germany">coal burning power station in Moorburg</a>, the only such coal burning facility of its kind to commence operations, in Western Europe in the 2010s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[344]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[345]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[346]</a></sup> Germany is likely to miss its 2020 emission reduction target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[347]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several studies suggest that it might be theoretically possible to cover a majority of world energy generation with new renewable sources.\nThe <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) has said that if governments were supportive, renewable energy supply could account for close to 80% of the world\'s energy use by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipccccc_348-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipccccc-348">[348]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Analysis in 2015 by professor and chair of Environmental Sustainability <a href="/wiki/Barry_Brook_(scientist)" title="Barry Brook (scientist)">Barry W. Brook</a> and his colleagues on the topic of replacing fossil fuels entirely, from the electric grid of the world, has determined that at the historically modest and proven-rate at which nuclear energy was added to and replaced fossil fuels in France and Sweden during each nation\'s building programs in the 1980s, nuclear energy could displace or remove fossil fuels from the electric grid completely within 10 years, "allow[ing] the world to meet the most stringent greenhouse-gas mitigation targets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journals.plos.org_349-0"><a href="#cite_note-journals.plos.org-349">[349]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[350]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a similar analysis, Brook had earlier determined that 50% of all <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">global energy</a>, that is not solely electricity, but transportation <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuels" title="Synthetic fuels">synthetic fuels</a> etc. could be generated within approximately 30 years, if the global nuclear fission build rate was identical to each of these nation\'s already proven installation rates in units of installed <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> per year, per unit of global <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDP" title="GDP">GDP</a> (GW/year/$).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_351-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-351">[351]</a></sup>\nThis is in contrast to the conceptual studies for a <i><a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a></i> world, which would require an orders of magnitude more costly global investment per year, which has no historical precedent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_352-0"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-352">[352]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[353]</a></sup> along with far greater land that would have to be devoted to the wind, wave and solar projects, and the inherent assumption that humanity will use less, and not more, energy in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_351-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-351">[351]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_352-1"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-352">[352]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_354-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-354">[354]</a></sup> As Brook notes, the "principal limitations on nuclear fission are not technical, economic or fuel-related, but are instead linked to complex issues of societal acceptance, fiscal and political inertia, and inadequate critical evaluation of the real-world constraints facing [the other] low-carbon alternatives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_351-2"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-351">[351]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In some countries which aim to burn a lot less fossil fuels, such as the UK, there is a lack of seasonal energy storage, so having renewables supply over 60% of electricity would be expensive. Whether interconnectors or new nuclear would be more expensive than taking renewables over 60% is currently (2019] being researched and debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[355]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nuclear power is comparable to, and in some cases lower, than many renewable energy sources in terms of lives lost per unit of electricity delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarkandyaWilkinson2007_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-MarkandyaWilkinson2007-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-without_the_hot_air_290-1"><a href="#cite_note-without_the_hot_air-290">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starfelt_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starfelt-356">[356]</a></sup>\nHowever, as opposed to renewable energy, conventional designs for nuclear reactors produce a smaller volume of manufacture and operations related waste, most notably, the intensely radioactive spent fuel that needs to be stored or reprocessed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[357]</a></sup>\nA nuclear plant also needs to be disassembled and removed and much of the disassembled nuclear plant needs to be stored as low level nuclear waste for a few decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[358]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In an EU wide 2018 assessment of progress in reducing <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions_per_capita" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions per capita">greenhouse gas emissions per capita</a>, France and Sweden were the only two large industrialized nations within the EU to receive a positive rating, as every other country received a "poor" to "very poor" grade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[359]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2018 analysis by <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a> argued that, to be much more cost-effective as they approach deep <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decarbonization" title="Decarbonization">decarbonization</a>, electricity systems should integrate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baseload" title="Baseload">baseload</a> low carbon resources, such as nuclear, with renewables, storage and demand response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT2018_360-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIT2018-360">[360]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nuclear power stations require approximately one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Square_kilometer" title="Square kilometer">square kilometer</a> of land per typical reactor whilst to produce the same electrical output, a wind farm requires 360 sq kilometers of land without obstructions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[361]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[362]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-363"><a href="#cite_note-363">[363]</a></sup> At the commonly proposed level of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acreage" title="Acreage">acreage</a> increase for inland wind farms, on the order of 1,000,000 sq km,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[364]</a></sup> environmentalists and <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">conservationists</a> have begun to question the global renewable energy expansion proposals, as they are opposed to the frequently controversial use of once forested land to situate renewable energy systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-365"><a href="#cite_note-365">[365]</a></sup> Seventy five academic conservationists signed a letter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[366]</a></sup> suggesting a more effective policy to mitigate climate change involving the <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation#Reforestation_and_afforestation" title="Climate change mitigation">reforestation</a> of this land proposed for renewable energy production, to its prior <a href="/wiki/Natural_landscape" title="Natural landscape">natural landscape</a>, by means of the native trees that previously inhabited it, in tandem with the lower land use footprint of nuclear energy, as the path to assure both the commitment to carbon emission reductions and to succeed with landscape <a href="/wiki/Rewilding_(conservation_biology)" title="Rewilding (conservation biology)">rewilding</a> programs that are part of the global <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_conservation" title="Wildlife conservation">native species protection and re-introduction</a> initiatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-367"><a href="#cite_note-367">[367]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[368]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-369"><a href="#cite_note-369">[369]</a></sup>\n</p><p>These, mostly biological scientists, argue that government commitments to increase renewable energy usage while simultaneously making commitments to expand areas of <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">biological conservation</a>, are two competing land use outcomes, in opposition to one another, that are increasingly coming into conflict. With the existing <a href="/wiki/Protected_area" title="Protected area">protected areas</a> for conservation at present regarded as insufficient to safeguard <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> "the conflict for space between energy production and habitat will remain one of the key future conservation issues to resolve."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[370]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[371]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[372]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate_on_nuclear_power">Debate on nuclear power</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">Nuclear power debate</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">Nuclear energy policy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pro-nuclear_movement" title="Pro-nuclear movement">Pro-nuclear movement</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">Anti-nuclear movement</a></div>\n<p>The nuclear power debate concerns the controversy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jstor.org_373-0"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-373">[373]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Reasonable_Bet_on_Nuclear_Power_374-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_Reasonable_Bet_on_Nuclear_Power-374">[374]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eleven_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-eleven-70">[70]</a></sup> which has surrounded the deployment and use of nuclear fission reactors to generate electricity from nuclear fuel for civilian purposes. The debate about nuclear power peaked during the 1970s and 1980s, when it "reached an intensity unprecedented in the history of technology controversies", in some countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marcuse.org_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-marcuse.org-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[375]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Proponents of nuclear energy regard it as a <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">sustainable energy</a> source that reduces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a> and increases <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">energy security</a> by decreasing dependence on imported energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_376-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-376">[376]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[377]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-378"><a href="#cite_note-378">[378]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/M._King_Hubbert" title="M. King Hubbert">M. King Hubbert</a>, who popularized the concept of <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>, saw oil as a resource that would run out and considered nuclear energy its replacement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-379"><a href="#cite_note-379">[379]</a></sup>\nProponents also claim that the present quantity of nuclear waste is small and can be reduced through the latest technology of newer reactors, and that the operational safety record of fission-electricity is unparalleled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[380]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Opponents believe that nuclear power poses many threats to people and the environment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Share_381-0"><a href="#cite_note-Share-381">[381]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NC_WARN_&#187;_Nuclear_Power_382-0"><a href="#cite_note-NC_WARN_&#187;_Nuclear_Power-382">[382]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sturgis_383-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sturgis-383">[383]</a></sup> such as the risk of nuclear weapons proliferation and terrorism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gierec_384-0"><a href="#cite_note-gierec-384">[384]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-protest_385-0"><a href="#cite_note-protest-385">[385]</a></sup> They also contend that reactors are complex machines where many things can and have gone wrong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bksenpol_386-0"><a href="#cite_note-bksenpol-386">[386]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-387"><a href="#cite_note-387">[387]</a></sup> In years past, they also argued that when all the energy-intensive stages of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_chain" title="Nuclear fuel chain">nuclear fuel chain</a> are considered, from uranium mining to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">nuclear decommissioning</a>, nuclear power is neither a low-carbon nor an economical electricity source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[388]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-389"><a href="#cite_note-389">[389]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-markd_390-0"><a href="#cite_note-markd-390">[390]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Arguments of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">economics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" title="Nuclear safety">safety</a> are used by both sides of the debate.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advanced_fission_reactor_designs">Advanced fission reactor designs</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">Generation IV reactor</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GenIVRoadmap-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="971" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/GenIVRoadmap-en.svg/440px-GenIVRoadmap-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/GenIVRoadmap-en.svg/660px-GenIVRoadmap-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/GenIVRoadmap-en.svg/880px-GenIVRoadmap-en.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GenIVRoadmap-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Generation IV roadmap from <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Current fission reactors in operation around the world are <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">second</a> or <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">third generation</a> systems, with most of the first-generation systems having been already retired.\nResearch into advanced <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">generation IV reactor</a> types was officially started by the Generation IV International Forum (GIF) based on eight technology goals, including to improve economics, safety, proliferation resistance, natural resource utilization and the ability to consume existing nuclear waste in the production of electricity.\nMost of these reactors differ significantly from current operating light water reactors, and are expected to be available for commercial construction after 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[391]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid_nuclear_fusion-fission">Hybrid nuclear fusion-fission</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion%E2%80%93fission_hybrid" title="Nuclear fusion&#8211;fission hybrid">Nuclear fusion&#8211;fission hybrid</a></div>\n<p>Hybrid nuclear power is a proposed means of generating power by use of a combination of nuclear fusion and fission processes. The concept dates to the 1950s, and was briefly advocated by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">Hans Bethe</a> during the 1970s, but largely remained unexplored until a revival of interest in 2009, due to delays in the realization of pure fusion. When a sustained nuclear fusion power plant is built, it has the potential to be capable of extracting all the fission energy that remains in spent fission fuel, reducing the volume of nuclear waste by orders of magnitude, and more importantly, eliminating all actinides present in the spent fuel, substances which cause security concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hybrid_392-0"><a href="#cite_note-hybrid-392">[392]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_fusion">Nuclear fusion</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_(9786811206).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2040" data-file-width="2040" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg/220px-U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg/330px-U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg/440px-U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_(9786811206).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic of the <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a> <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">tokamak</a> under construction in France.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">Nuclear fusion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">Fusion power</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">Nuclear fusion</a> reactions have the potential to be safer and generate less radioactive waste than fission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[393]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WorldEnergyCouncil_394-0"><a href="#cite_note-WorldEnergyCouncil-394">[394]</a></sup>\nThese reactions appear potentially viable, though technically quite difficult and have yet to be created on a scale that could be used in a functional power plant.\nFusion power has been under theoretical and experimental investigation since the 1950s.\n</p><p>Several experimental nuclear fusion reactors and facilities exist.\nThe largest and most ambitious international nuclear fusion project currently in progress is <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a>, a large <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">tokamak</a> under construction in France.\nITER is planned to pave the way for commercial fusion power by demonstrating self-sustained nuclear fusion reactions with positive energy gain.\nConstruction of the ITER facility began in 2007, but the project has run into many delays and budget overruns.\nThe facility is now not expected to begin operations until the year 2027&#8212;11 years after initially anticipated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-395"><a href="#cite_note-395">[395]</a></sup> A follow on commercial nuclear fusion power station, <a href="/wiki/DEMOnstration_Power_Station" title="DEMOnstration Power Station">DEMO</a>, has been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITERorg_396-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITERorg-396">[396]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EFDA_Activities_397-0"><a href="#cite_note-EFDA_Activities-397">[397]</a></sup> There are also suggestions for a power plant based upon a different fusion approach, that of an <a href="/wiki/Inertial_fusion_power_plant" title="Inertial fusion power plant">inertial fusion power plant</a>.\n</p><p>Fusion powered electricity generation was initially believed to be readily achievable, as fission-electric power had been. However, the extreme requirements for continuous reactions and <a href="/wiki/Plasma_containment" title="Plasma containment">plasma containment</a> led to projections being extended by several decades. In 2010, more than 60 years after the first attempts, commercial power production was still believed to be unlikely before 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITERorg_396-1"><a href="#cite_note-ITERorg-396">[396]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="448" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="28"
